I would create a flip,&#x201D; she says. &#x201C;That is where I live.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Her first boyfriend, Kris Funn, whom she met when she was 19, played the upright bass, and she sat in bars for hours, watching him and his friends play. She joked to me that she was a &#x201C;jazz wife&#x201D; but also admitted that she received an unexpected education: She learned to listen to music, to get a feel for it. Eventually the couple broke up, but Funn encouraged Kelela to trust her instincts and not be intimidated by her lack of formal music training.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">By that time, Kelela was a student at American University, studying international studies and sociology. &#x201C;I was the only brown girl, all the time, talking about African politics,&#x201D; she told me. &#x201C;I thought I was going to be an academic. In my head, I am supposed to be a college graduate. I wanted to finish. But I was not motivated to sit there and do that paper. I had a lot of resistance.&#x201D; She felt alienated by the program. She dropped out.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">This was in 2006, and synthpop, epitomized by bands like the Knife, was trending. She began recording in a punk house in Washington, a city with a hard-core lineage that included acts like Fugazi and Bad Brains. She thrived in an environment devoid of rules. &#x201C;What&#x2019;s so beautiful about punk culture,&#x201D; Kelela says, &#x201C;is that the whole idea is that you don&#x2019;t have to be perfect. Just try. Let&#x2019;s just try to make it ourselves.&#x201D; At first she sang over indie rock, but it didn&#x2019;t feel authentic to her. She wanted to experiment with electronic music &#x2014; &#x201C;not real instruments,&#x201D; she says.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">She spent hours on MySpace, scrolling through pages of music and listening to instrumentals. She recorded herself singing over sounds she liked. Then she would send the artist her sample, along with an invitation to collaborate. Two notable electronic producers agreed, including Daedelus, who featured her on a track. At the same time, a friend introduced her to the electro duo Teengirl Fantasy, and they created a song. Will Boston, a founder of the music label Fade to Mind, heard their collaboration and was impressed by what he described to me as her &#x201C;wealth of startling honesty, felt through her vocals.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">By then, Kelela was living in Los Angeles, and Boston brought her a thumb drive of sounds from the label and its British counterpart, Night Slugs. Kelela spent the next several days poring over the files, improvising lyrics over the sounds she liked, turning them into songs. She loved the otherworldliness of the instrumentals &#x2014; staccato mixes that used sound effects like tinkling glass and guns reloading over drum machines. The music complemented the gossamer scales she likes to sing in. It was &#x201C;exactly what I&#x2019;d been looking for,&#x201D; she says. Two of the songs she produced during this time were on the mixtape she released in 2013. Kelela took care to describe her role in developing the mixtape, to make certain I knew how active she was in it &#x2014; perhaps to counter the idea that she isn&#x2019;t self-made or in charge of her own sound. &#x201C;The first thing people want to take away from me is my agency,&#x201D; she told me.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            \n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  <figure\n  id=\"media-100000004967849\"\n  class=\"media photo horizontal jumbo\">\n  <div class=\"image\">\n    <img src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music5-kelela2/12music5-kelela2-master180-v2.jpg\"\n    data-ratio=\"0.6\"\n    data-pattern=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music5-kelela2/12music5-kelela2-{{size}}-v2.jpg\"\n    data-widths='[{\"slug\":\"blogSmallInline\",\"size\":151},{\"slug\":\"master180\",\"size\":180},{\"slug\":\"blog225\",\"size\":225},{\"slug\":\"tmagSF\",\"size\":362},{\"slug\":\"blog427\",\"size\":427},{\"slug\":\"blog480\",\"size\":480},{\"slug\":\"master495\",\"size\":495},{\"slug\":\"blog533\",\"size\":533},{\"slug\":\"tmagArticle\",\"size\":592},{\"slug\":\"articleLarge\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"slide\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"popup\",\"size\":650},{\"slug\":\"master675\",\"size\":675},{\"slug\":\"jumbo\",\"size\":1024},{\"slug\":\"master1050\",\"size\":1050},{\"slug\":\"superJumbo\",\"size\":2048}]'\n    itemprop=\"url\"/>\n  </div>\n  <figcaption class=\"caption\">\n    <span class=\"caption-text\">Kelela performing at the 2016 S\u00f3nar festival in Barcelona.</span>\n    <span class=\"credit\">Xavi Torrent/Redferns</span>\n  </figcaption>\n</figure>\n\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Alex Sushon, an electronic producer who goes by Bok Bok, was one of the artists Kelela met as she was working on her mixtape, and these days, she tends to work mostly with British producers like him, possibly because they&#x2019;ve been pushing the boundaries of R.&amp;B. more aggressively by blending it with grime, the East London style of dance music. Electronica, Sushon told me, is referential in the same way that R.&amp;B. tends to be. &#x201C;That&#x2019;s how we think about music, inserting small samples from other genres, little shortcuts that are saying something by playing something,&#x201D; he says. Because of the internet, he explained, musicians can share references more easily than they did in the past. Google, YouTube and SoundCloud make it easy. Sushon compares this dynamic to &#x201C;memes, but in musical form.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">The closest analogy is sampling, but it&#x2019;s more complex than that. I watched Kelela and her D.J. perform this trick onstage, during one of their songs in Strasbourg. While she was singing &#x201C;The High,&#x201D; a fuzzed-out, gritty ode to desire, Sih mixed in a few seconds of a 2002 song by Tweet called &#x201C;Oops (Oh My),&#x201D; and Kelela segued into its vocals. Here, suddenly, was the thrilling flicker of a decade-old hit that had almost entirely faded from popular culture, tucked into her own noir love song. It was the perfect encapsulation of Kelela&#x2019;s odd 21st-century gift for taking familiar sounds and repurposing them in a new context, a kind of lyrical d&#xE9;j&#xE0; vu.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><strong>After the show</strong>, back at her Strasbourg Airbnb, Kelela changed into oversize gray sweatpants and a black button-down crop top, and padded into the kitchen in white slippers. She plugged in an electric kettle and made another cup of ginger tea as our conversation turned to her debut album. I expected her to talk about its sound, but she wanted to speak about the intention behind it. She described the album as &#x201C;something that speaks to power.&#x201D; When I asked her to elaborate, she paused, then said: &#x201C;I&#x2019;m not talking to black people, to be honest. I don&#x2019;t need to tell black people that R.&amp;B. is deep. It&#x2019;s about the music industry. When I say the white gaze, I&#x2019;m not just talking about white people at my show. I like that. I like playing to mixed crowds. For me, it&#x2019;s really just the way I&#x2019;m treated by people of positions of power who seemingly hold the key.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">The dynamics that bothered Kelela in college didn&#x2019;t disappear when she became an artist &#x2014; if anything, they intensified. As a student, she was introduced to black academics and feminists like Audre Lorde, Bell Hooks and Angela Davis. These women helped her make sense of the racial and sexist forces that shape the world, and she still turns to them to navigate the music industry. She internalized their insistence to not be apologetic for her womanhood or blackness and not be debilitated by exclusion.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Sprawled on the bed in the apartment&#x2019;s master bedroom, Kelela told me that Beyonc&#xE9;&#x2019;s loss at the Grammys depressed her and made her question the metrics of success for mainstream musicians. &#x201C;If that&#x2019;s the highest we can achieve in that framework, it doesn&#x2019;t even make sense to try,&#x201D; she told me. A notebook was open to a blank page on the night stand, alongside &#x201C;B Jenkins,&#x201D; a Fred Moten poetry book that explores the interstitial space among jazz, black aesthetics and politics. Moten&#x2019;s work concerns itself with the &#x201C;resistance of the object&#x201D; and the way that blackness refuses commodification. Kelela is aware of how artists like her get co-opted, morphed into something symbolic that they no longer control, and is determined to avoid it.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">I had already heard the lengths to which she would go to prevent this from happening. The first night we met, I asked her how she managed expectations as an artist in an age of hyperconsumption. I mostly meant her reserve on social media, despite the disturbingly insistent demands in her Twitter and Instagram mentions for her next release. Instead, she described an encounter with Fendi, the Italian luxury brand, which invited her to perform at its new headquarters in Rome to celebrate the start of a new website aimed at millennials. Researching the headquarters online, she discovered that the Palazzo della Civilt&#xE0; Italiana, the impressive white marble colosseum that Fendi had taken over, was built to be a propaganda tool under Benito Mussolini. &#x201C;I&#x2019;m Ethiopian,&#x201D; she reminded me: Mussolini invaded Ethiopia in 1935, just before World War II. She didn&#x2019;t want her body, her blackness, to be used by a brand, even a big fashion label like Fendi, as a proxy for cool in this context. She asked Fendi representatives to agree to release a statement addressing her concerns as a condition of her involvement. &#x201C;That flipped the whole damn script,&#x201D; she said. They&#x2019;re still negotiating the partnership, but the interaction speaks to how Kelela views her role: &#x201C;If an artist says something, people listen,&#x201D; she told me. She sees herself as someone who can wield her status as a celebrity to catalyze change.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">As the evening wound down, Kelela invited me to get comfortable and listen to some of her new tracks. She gave me earbuds and left me alone to listen. (When I pressed her about a release date, she made a coquettish face and demurred, saying the songs were still being mixed. In reality, she just signed with Warp Records, which will take over the release of the album.) Earlier in the night, she said that as politically aware as she feels she is, she didn&#x2019;t make an album that addresses those views. &#x201C;There was a period after I finished all the songs, I was nervous because I was like: There&#x2019;s nothing about my experience as a black woman overtly in this. But I could never not make anything from any other place.&#x201D; The songs instead deal with the agony of falling out of love and the ecstasy of finding it anew. Her voice is as pretty as ever, rising and crashing like cresting waves over beats that swing from a druggy drone to throbbing bass lines perfect for dance-floor grinding. In their own way, they are a quiet protest: They feel radical in the way a Kerry James Marshall painting or a Ntozake Shange poem expresses the humanity and beauty of black life. &#x201C;All of that is happening in a world that does not want me,&#x201D; she said. &#x201C;It is a safe space for us to feel, and not necessarily for anyone else.&#x201D;&#x2666;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><em>Jenna Wortham is a staff writer for the magazine.</em></p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n          <div class=\"read-more\">Read more</div>\n        </div>\n      </section>\n      \n      <div class=\"m-ad-container\"></div>\n      \n    \n      <section id=\"/kungs-vs-cookin-on-3-burners-this-girl\" data-id=\"kungs-vs-cookin-on-3-burners-this-girl\"\n        class=\"m-section  kungs-vs-cookin-on-3-burners\">\n        \n        <header class=\"m-header\">\n          <div class=\"m-header-meta\">\n            <h2 class=\"m-number\">9<span class=\"dot\">.<span></h2>\n            \n            <h2 class=\"m-headline\">\n              <span class=\"m-player\" data-slug=\"kungs-vs-cookin-on-3-burners-this-girl\">\n                <audio class=\"m-audio\" src=\"https://p.scdn.co/mp3-preview/9f5ce3d3a3cd34c6ef54a9557a3053af9c7a5419?cid=null\"></audio>\n              </span>\n              <span class=\"m-headline-text\">This Girl</span>\n            </h2>\n            <h4 class=\"m-artist\">Kungs vs. Cookin\u2019 on 3 Burners</h4>\n            \n            <div class=\"m-header-footer\">\n              <p class=\"m-leadin\">An earworm that nearly eluded its audience.</p>\n              <p class=\"m-byline\">By LYDIA KIESLING</p>\n            </div>\n            <div class=\"tools-wrapper\">\n              <div class=\"m-sharetools\" data-shares=\"facebook,twitter\"\n                data-url=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2017/03/09/magazine/25-songs-that-tell-us-where-music-is-going.html#/kungs-vs-cookin-on-3-burners-this-girl\"\n                data-title=\"What Kungs vs. Cookin\u2019 on 3 Burners's work can tell us about where music is headed.\"\n                data-description=\"\"></div>\n              \n              <a class=\"m-full-track-link\" href=\"https://open.spotify.com/track/1A8j067qyiNwQnZT0bzUpZ\" target=\"_blank\">\n                <img class=\"spotify-icon\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2017/03/03/music2017/assets/icons/spotify.svg\" />Full Track\n              </a>\n              \n            </div>\n            \n          </div>\n        </header>\n        \n        <div class=\"content \">\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">The song, &#x201C;This Girl,&#x201D; is a result of the (at the time) 19-year-old French D.J. and producer Valentin Brunel, alias Kungs, trawling YouTube videos in his bedroom and finding an obscure 2009 soul-revival tune by the Australian band Cookin&#x2019; on 3 Burners. Brunel remixed the song, speeding it up into what he calls a &#x201C;happy house track.&#x201D; With the aid of Spotify, the song soon became a viral hit, topping charts across the globe and leaving no one more surprised than its respective creators.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Kungs&#x2019;s remix was conceived on YouTube, and YouTube also contains a record of its charms. <a href=\"https://youtu.be/2Y6Nne8RvaA\">The video</a>, which has been viewed over 200 million times and depicts a summer romance on a Greek isle, is followed by hundreds of comments from jubilant global citizens who have finally trapped their earworm. &#x201C;This Girl&#x201D; now hovers around No.20 on the Shazam Hall of Fame, meaning that more than 13 million users of the app were similarly desperate to find it. For nine weeks, it was the most Shazammed song in the world.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">What is it about &#x201C;This Girl&#x201D;? The rich vocals of Kylie Auldist, the singer for Cookin&#x2019; on 3 Burners? The retro, cheerful, almost cloying guitar riff? Cynics might say that Kungs left the heavy lifting to Auldist and her bandmates, but this diminishes Kungs&#x2019;s inspired additions: his trumpets and his house beat, crucial to the ecstatic danciness of the new version. The result is youthful magic, the aural version of dancing until dawn with a boy you just met. Of smoking cigarettes on a rooftop all hot summer night.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">These days, an enterprising 19-year-old can browse YouTube, find something that catches his fancy, transform it and broadcast it to the world. An algorithm can anticipate my taste so precisely that it serves up the song I&#x2019;ve been dying to hear. There&#x2019;s something about the timing: &#x201C;This Girl&#x201D; is upbeat and joyful and nostalgic when the news has been a grim parade. The same year the video&#x2019;s young lovers frolicked on the Mediterranean, some 5,000 people died trying to cross the same body of water. Our atmosphere is on track to become one long hot summer night. In harrowing times, this earworm asks little and gives a lot. It&#x2019;s an echo of better times &#x2014; and one you can dance to.&#x2666;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><em>Lydia Kiesling is the editor of The Millions.</em></p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n          <div class=\"read-more\">Read more</div>\n        </div>\n      </section>\n      \n    \n      <section id=\"/church-of-misery-make-them-die-slowly-john-george-haigh\" data-id=\"church-of-misery-make-them-die-slowly-john-george-haigh\"\n        class=\"m-section  church-of-misery\">\n        \n        <header class=\"m-header\">\n          <div class=\"m-header-meta\">\n            <h2 class=\"m-number\">10<span class=\"dot\">.<span></h2>\n            \n            <h2 class=\"m-headline\">\n              <span class=\"m-player\" data-slug=\"church-of-misery-make-them-die-slowly-john-george-haigh\">\n                <audio class=\"m-audio\" src=\"https://p.scdn.co/mp3-preview/f7b8799a4f0642d4337d5eddd91bb4137dfd5232?cid=null\"></audio>\n              </span>\n              <span class=\"m-headline-text\">Make Them Die Slowly (John George Haigh)</span>\n            </h2>\n            <h4 class=\"m-artist\">Church of Misery</h4>\n            \n            <div class=\"m-header-footer\">\n              <p class=\"m-leadin\">Very good music for very bad feelings.</p>\n              <p class=\"m-byline\">By JOHN DARNIELLE</p>\n            </div>\n            <div class=\"tools-wrapper\">\n              <div class=\"m-sharetools\" data-shares=\"facebook,twitter\"\n                data-url=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2017/03/09/magazine/25-songs-that-tell-us-where-music-is-going.html#/church-of-misery-make-them-die-slowly-john-george-haigh\"\n                data-title=\"What Church of Misery's work can tell us about where music is headed.\"\n                data-description=\"\"></div>\n              \n              <a class=\"m-full-track-link\" href=\"https://open.spotify.com/track/08O2pHuPdcJ5PoxFqbpxLO\" target=\"_blank\">\n                <img class=\"spotify-icon\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2017/03/03/music2017/assets/icons/spotify.svg\" />Full Track\n              </a>\n              \n            </div>\n            \n          </div>\n        </header>\n        \n        <div class=\"content \">\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Sometimes you just want to kill somebody, you know? Really end their life: make mourners of their friends and family, make orphans of their children, leave a hole in the world where a person once was. You don&#x2019;t feel this way all the time &#x2014; you&#x2019;re not a monster. But sometimes you do. Maybe this didn&#x2019;t always happen; maybe you can remember a time when you not only didn&#x2019;t want to kill anybody but also hoped, moreover, to never even know what it felt like to want such an awful thing. But you were a child then, probably, and poets are always making hay about how you can&#x2019;t get back to that condition in this world. You&#x2019;re not a kid anymore, and every once in a while &#x2014; not every day, except maybe sometimes for several days in a row &#x2014; you want to kill somebody.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Maybe you don&#x2019;t. I don&#x2019;t know. But if you do, when you do, maybe sometimes it kind of gets away from you, right? Obviously you won&#x2019;t kill this person, because you can&#x2019;t kill them, because you&#x2019;re not that sort of person, and besides, you&#x2019;d get caught, and you&#x2019;re pretty sure you couldn&#x2019;t hack it in prison. Would you cover your tracks? Try to hide the body? Go into hiding and hear about yourself on the news? Walk through the doors of the police station and turn yourself in? You think about these things when you want to kill somebody. You have the occasional dream about them. They&#x2019;re not pleasant thoughts, and it&#x2019;s not a pleasant dream.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">What&#x2019;s your damage? How did you get like this? Brain chemistry? Trauma? Read too many stories about Ozzy Osbourne biting the heads off bats when you were a kid? It doesn&#x2019;t matter. Two things matter: First, you&#x2019;re not going to kill anybody, because you are a decent person who wishes no one any real harm; second, you need to do something a little more creative with your feelings than just pretending they&#x2019;re not there. For some people, that means hitting the gym. For others, it means a stereo with a volume knob.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            \n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  <figure\n  id=\"media-100000004969965\"\n  class=\"media photo vertical jumbo\">\n  <div class=\"image\">\n    <img src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music15-misery/12mag-12misery-t_CA0-master180.jpg\"\n    data-ratio=\"1.2055555555555555\"\n    data-pattern=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music15-misery/12mag-12misery-t_CA0-{{size}}.jpg\"\n    data-widths='[{\"slug\":\"blogSmallInline\",\"size\":151},{\"slug\":\"master180\",\"size\":180},{\"slug\":\"blog225\",\"size\":225},{\"slug\":\"tmagSF\",\"size\":362},{\"slug\":\"slide\",\"size\":415},{\"slug\":\"popup\",\"size\":415},{\"slug\":\"blog427\",\"size\":427},{\"slug\":\"blog480\",\"size\":480},{\"slug\":\"master495\",\"size\":495},{\"slug\":\"blog533\",\"size\":533},{\"slug\":\"tmagArticle\",\"size\":592},{\"slug\":\"articleLarge\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"master675\",\"size\":675},{\"slug\":\"jumbo\",\"size\":849},{\"slug\":\"master1050\",\"size\":1050},{\"slug\":\"superJumbo\",\"size\":1698}]'\n    itemprop=\"url\"/>\n  </div>\n  <figcaption class=\"caption\">\n    <span class=\"caption-text\"></span>\n    <span class=\"credit\">Illustration by St. Francis Elevator Ride. Source image: Evina Schmidova.</span>\n  </figcaption>\n</figure>\n\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Heavy metal has been providing people with catharsis for nearly 50 years. There&#x2019;s more to metal than that, of course, which ought to go without saying &#x2014; not every metalhead harbors some hideous inner sadist. When I listen to death metal, it&#x2019;s not for the splatter-gore violence of its themes but for the breathtaking proficiency and relentless musical curiosity of the musicians who play it: To even get your foot in death metal&#x2019;s door, you have to be awfully good at your instrument. But Church of Misery doesn&#x2019;t play death metal; it plays stoner doom (the band itself prefers &#x201C;doom,&#x201D; without the modifier), and I don&#x2019;t listen to it for the pleasure of immersion in technical wizardry. I listen to it because of how it makes me feel.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Who&#x2019;s Church of Misery? Chiefly, they&#x2019;re the brainchild of the bassist Tatsu Mikami. They&#x2019;ve been putting out records since the late 1990s. They have six studio efforts, numerous EPs and a live album to their credit, and every song on every album except one takes, as its theme, a known serial killer. (Their first studio recording was made before they settled on their thematic focus and wasn&#x2019;t officially released for years.) Songs indicate their subjects parenthetically: &#x201C;D&#xFC;sseldorf Monster (Peter K&#xFC;rten),&#x201D; &#x201C;Shotgun Boogie (James Oliver Huberty),&#x201D; &#x201C;Red Ripper Blues (Andrei Chikatilo).&#x201D; Some of their subjects are widely infamous &#x2014; Charles Manson, Ted Bundy, the Heaven&#x2019;s Gate cult. Others are so obscure that only true crime buffs are likely to recognize their names. Look them up at your peril: These are people whose crimes will give you nightmares.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">&#x201C;Make Them Die Slowly&#x201D; is the second song on the band&#x2019;s latest album, &#x201C;And Then There Were None ... ,&#x201D; and it is impossibly heavy. It begins with a thudding kick drum all alone, with the central guitar riff ambling in murderously after two bars &#x2014; a figure that lurches methodically through three five-note patterns to resolve on three descending chords that land like boulders being dropped on a house. My iTunes play count shows that I listened to it more than I listened to any song in 2016 except for drafts of songs I was writing myself. Scott Carlson of the legendary Repulsion sings it; the 2016 incarnation of the band was essentially a reboot, with Mikami the only original member. Carlson&#x2019;s feel for metal forms is instinctive and inerrant; when he sings the initial chorus &#x2014; &#x201C;I&#x2019;ll hang you high and drain your blood/sip on the nectar while your bones reduce to sludge&#x201D; &#x2014; it&#x2019;s as if all that stuff about Ozzy had been true: Here&#x2019;s a guy gleefully recounting obscure, unthinkable real-world crimes as if they were fond memories of his time in varsity football. It&#x2019;s seductive and irresistible. It has a cowbell. You can bang your head and sing along.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">I have spent a fair bit of idle time over the years wondering what it says about me that I want to indulge this mood at least a few times a week for the rest of my life, occasionally at earsplitting volumes in clubs. I don&#x2019;t think I&#x2019;m secretly a murderer; I&#x2019;m a liberal softy. I can&#x2019;t even eat animals. When I was young, if I heard something that sounded too celebratory of death, it terrified me. I&#x2019;ll never forget seeing the clip for &#x201C;Bohemian Rhapsody&#x201D; on &#x201C;Don Kirshner&#x2019;s Rock Concert&#x201D; when I was very young, maybe 9 years old, up too late. I listened to Freddie Mercury sing the verse about how he&#x2019;d just killed a man by shooting him point blank in the head &#x2014; although I thought he was speaking in the third person rather than the vocative, that it was his &#x201C;mama&#x201D; who&#x2019;d just killed a man &#x2014; and, horrified, I turned off the television. Over time, though, that feeling &#x2014; the shock, the revulsion &#x2014; became an object of interior curiosity: What&#x2019;s this about? How much time can I spend with it? What part of me is it? What does it look like up close?</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">The cheap answer is something about the cathartic value of transgression, etc., all old hoary stuff I don&#x2019;t really buy into anymore. The truer answer, for me, is that sometimes you really wanna kill somebody. Naturally, you won&#x2019;t. You mustn&#x2019;t. It would be wrong. You try not to do wrong. But if you spend a little time in the presence of a perfect groove contemplating the wrong directly without moralizing about it, you can ride the feeling in safety and go in as deep as you want, emerging later not wanting to kill anybody. When this has become a pattern, it&#x2019;s an immense release, as addictive as the pellets they would give us every so often if we were rats in labs.&#x2666;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><em>John Darnielle is a musician and the author of &#x201C;Universal Harvester.&#x201D;</em></p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n          <div class=\"read-more\">Read more</div>\n        </div>\n      </section>\n      \n      <div class=\"m-ad-container\"></div>\n      \n    \n      <section id=\"/mica-levi-oliver-coates-barok-main\" data-id=\"mica-levi-oliver-coates-barok-main\"\n        class=\"m-section feature mica-levi-oliver-coates\">\n        \n        <header class=\"m-header\">\n          <div class=\"m-header-meta\">\n            <h2 class=\"m-number\">11<span class=\"dot\">.<span></h2>\n            \n            <h2 class=\"m-headline\">\n              <span class=\"m-player\" data-slug=\"mica-levi-oliver-coates-barok-main\">\n                <audio class=\"m-audio\" src=\"https://p.scdn.co/mp3-preview/3fd65bce90f2de51ac2a678c741e4fa8ae0fba5a?cid=null\"></audio>\n              </span>\n              <span class=\"m-headline-text\">Barok Main</span>\n            </h2>\n            \n            <div class=\"m-header-footer\">\n              <p class=\"m-leadin\">Two composers beyond category.</p>\n              <p class=\"m-byline\">By RYAN BRADLEY</p>\n            </div>\n            <div class=\"tools-wrapper\">\n              <div class=\"m-sharetools\" data-shares=\"facebook,twitter\"\n                data-url=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2017/03/09/magazine/25-songs-that-tell-us-where-music-is-going.html#/mica-levi-oliver-coates-barok-main\"\n                data-title=\"What Mica Levi & Oliver Coates's work can tell us about where music is headed.\"\n                data-description=\"\"></div>\n              \n              <a class=\"m-full-track-link\" href=\"https://open.spotify.com/track/2Dy1MaumORP1kh1sHDXi0w\" target=\"_blank\">\n                <img class=\"spotify-icon\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2017/03/03/music2017/assets/icons/spotify.svg\" />Full Track\n              </a>\n              \n            </div>\n            \n              <h4 class=\"m-artist\">Mica Levi & Oliver Coates</h4>\n            \n          </div>\n        </header>\n        \n        <div class=\"content \">\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><span class=\"dropcap\">O</span>n a gray winter day in London, the composers Mica Levi and Oliver Coates met in a high-beamed former hat factory &#x2014; the offices of Levi&#x2019;s manager &#x2014; and climbed up to a loft space with an excellent stereo system. They had agreed to listen to &#x201C;Remain Calm,&#x201D; an album of experimental music that, more than a year ago, they improvised over the course of a single day. Each had moved on to new gigs since the album&#x2019;s November release, writing new work for different orchestras around England and Europe, and this was the first time since then that they&#x2019;d listened to it together. Coates sat on the edge of a couch; Levi took a chair; each looked expectant, borderline anxious.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">It had been a busy year. Levi is also a producer and D.J. who sings and plays guitar, most recently in the pop-art trio Micachu and the Shapes. She has written two movie scores; the second, for the biopic &#x201C;Jackie,&#x201D; was nominated for an Academy Award. Coates has scored films, too, but is better known for his work as a cellist. He contributed to Radiohead&#x2019;s &#x201C;A Moon Shaped Pool,&#x201D; released last May, and in the same month put out his own &#x201C;Upstepping,&#x201D; a very danceable record of house music performed almost entirely on his cello.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            \n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  <figure\n  id=\"media-100000004967584\"\n  class=\"media photo horizontal jumbo\">\n  <div class=\"image\">\n    <img src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music4-LEVI/12music4-LEVI-master180-v2.jpg\"\n    data-ratio=\"0.6666666666666666\"\n    data-pattern=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music4-LEVI/12music4-LEVI-{{size}}-v2.jpg\"\n    data-widths='[{\"slug\":\"blogSmallInline\",\"size\":151},{\"slug\":\"master180\",\"size\":180},{\"slug\":\"blog225\",\"size\":225},{\"slug\":\"tmagSF\",\"size\":362},{\"slug\":\"blog427\",\"size\":427},{\"slug\":\"blog480\",\"size\":480},{\"slug\":\"master495\",\"size\":495},{\"slug\":\"blog533\",\"size\":533},{\"slug\":\"tmagArticle\",\"size\":592},{\"slug\":\"articleLarge\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"slide\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"popup\",\"size\":650},{\"slug\":\"master675\",\"size\":675},{\"slug\":\"jumbo\",\"size\":1024},{\"slug\":\"master1050\",\"size\":1050},{\"slug\":\"superJumbo\",\"size\":2048}]'\n    itemprop=\"url\"/>\n  </div>\n  <figcaption class=\"caption\">\n    <span class=\"caption-text\"></span>\n    <span class=\"credit\">Erik Madigan Heck for The New York Times. Grooming: Annabel McCrory.</span>\n  </figcaption>\n</figure>\n\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">&#x201C;Remain Calm&#x201D; is an odd creation, even by their standards. Its 13 tracks, some less than a minute in length, jump from beat-heavy, densely layered and looped orchestrations to atmospheric and spacey noodlings. Often, Coates&#x2019;s cello seems to emerge, pure and acoustic, out of a stew of sonic burbles. It is a sketchbook in which every figure gestures toward newer, more exciting ideas to come, outlining musical rules (a key, a beat, a melody) one minute only to abandon them in the next.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Before they listened to the record, Coates reached into his backpack and pulled out a coloring book. He showed Levi one of the images he had colored in, a mandala filled with bright blues and greens, thin wisps of gold, bursts of coral pink. Levi leaned in for a closer look, drawing her finger across the page. &#x201C;Bruv, this is nuts,&#x201D; she said.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">&#x201C;It&#x2019;s what I wish composing was,&#x201D; he said. &#x201C;The form preordained.&#x201D; Levi nodded in agreement. It was a visual cantus firmus, she said: a fixed melody providing a structure for a limited range of improvisation. They had each studied classical composition: Coates at the Royal Academy of Music, where he received the highest marks in the school&#x2019;s history, Levi at Guildhall School of Music and Drama, where, at 21, she was commissioned to compose a piece for the London Philharmonic.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">The first song began, Coates playing a solo figure, then Levi&#x2019;s electronic textures trickling in, like an orchestra tuning up. The pair sat in silence, pleased enough but also distracted. A few tracks later, Coates looked up at Levi, who was looking at his mandala. &#x201C;I&#x2019;d like for it to be music,&#x201D; he said. It seemed like a familiar conversation. Levi massaged her temples, thinking, listening. &#x201C;You just get a set of rules,&#x201D; she said. &#x201C;Each color could be a different pitch.&#x201D; Coates frowned. The idea of &#x201C;algorithmic music&#x201D; troubled him. Maybe, Levi said, you set up the rules and then find a way to break them; color inside the lines, so to speak, and then scribble a face over the results. Coates liked it. The breaking of the rule, he said, is &#x201C;what makes a moment, musically.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">In fact, he added, his coloring was loaded with mistakes already, but the mistakes were what made the thing come together, in a subtle way. He turned the page, exposing the blots where the pen ink bled through to the other side and the sharp lines of the pattern were barely visible. He said they reminded him of one of Levi&#x2019;s works.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Coates and Levi met almost a decade ago. Coates had come to perform student string quartets for a class Levi was taking, and he was struck by her compositions. They were written so well, he said, &#x201C;that you knew immediately what you needed to do to make them come to life.&#x201D; From then on, the two stayed in touch. Coates sent Levi a video by the electronic producer Daniel Lopatin, also known as Oneohtrix Point Never; Levi sent Coates a mixtape she made with some tracks by Harry Partch, a composer who created new musical scales and built his own instruments.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">In 2011, Coates was working on a commission for the London Sinfonietta and suggested a collaboration with Micachu and the Shapes, who were already trying to, in Levi&#x2019;s words, &#x201C;smudge a whole lot of things together.&#x201D; For the Sinfonietta collaboration, Levi and her bandmates, following in Partch&#x2019;s footsteps, deployed a new instrument she had built with a friend called the chopper &#x2014; a hollow box with strings on top and a rotating wheel to pluck them, like a turntable that strummed. The live album that resulted, filled with wobbly, off-kilter strings, was called &#x201C;Chopped and Screwed&#x201D; (a nod to screw music, the Houston hip-hop style). It landed in the hands of the filmmaker Jonathan Glazer, who was looking for someone to score &#x201C;Under the Skin,&#x201D; his film about an alien exploring earth in a woman&#x2019;s body. He wanted an experienced composer who had never written music for a movie, someone who would come at the task differently. &#x201C;I think he wanted someone who was, you know, cheap,&#x201D; Levi said.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            \n            <figure\n  id=\"graphic-100000004976464\"\n  class=\"media graphic size-300\"\n  data-minimum-width=\"300\" >\n  <div class=\"interactive interactive-embedded\"\n       style=\"max-width: 300px\">\n    \n    <figcaption class=\"interactive-caption\">\n      <h2 class=\"interactive-headline\"></h2>\n      <h4 class=\"interactive-summary\"></h4>\n    </figcaption>\n    \n    <div class=\"interactive-graphic\"><script>function getFlexData() { return {\"data\":{\"quote\":\"The breaking of the rule is \u2018what makes a moment, musically.\u2019\",\"attribution\":\"\"}}; }var NYTD=NYTD || {}; NYTD.FlexTypes = NYTD.FlexTypes || []; NYTD.FlexTypes.push({\"target\":\"FT100000004976464\",\"type\":\"Pull Quote\",\"data\":{\"quote\":\"The breaking of the rule is \u2018what makes a moment, musically.\u2019\",\"attribution\":\"\"}});</script><link href=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2015/02/10/mag-pullquote-redesign/assets/style.css\" type=\"text/css\" rel=\"stylesheet\">\r\n\r\n<script>\r\n  var NYTG_ASSETS = \"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2015/02/10/mag-pullquote-redesign/b585ac490ed3103c57423bc4f581ffac30ebc5df/\",\r\n      NYTG_BIG_ASSETS = \"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2015/02/10/mag-pullquote-redesign/assets/\",\r\n      _gaq = _gaq || [];\r\n  require(['foundation/main'], function() {\r\n    require([\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2015/02/10/mag-pullquote-redesign/assets/build.js\"]); // generated from src/script.js\r\n  });\r\n</script><div id=\"FT100000004976464\"></div></div>\n    <div class=\"footer\">\n      <div class=\"interactive-notes\"></div>\n      <div class=\"interactive-source\"></div>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</figure>\n\n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">For 10 months, she worked on almost nothing else, worried that if she listened to anything &#x2014; particularly another soundtrack &#x2014; she would unintentionally steal from it. She focused instead on the challenge of scoring the emotional development of the film&#x2019;s unknowable alien, played by Scarlett Johansson. Levi&#x2019;s stark themes all spring from a dense void, abuzz in discordant strings (including Coates on cello), then synthetic and hollow with muffled drums and distant drones. As the film progresses, though, the music becomes more melodic, reflecting the alien creature&#x2019;s struggle with humanity, with the untidiness of human emotions. The soundtrack is unsettling, but also strangely empathetic.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Levi describes much of her work as mixtapes. For &#x201C;Jackie,&#x201D; she imagined classy melancholy, loads of strings, the occasional flute, clarinet and piano &#x2014; the kind of music that Jacqueline Kennedy might listen to, a mixtape for private mourning made for a woman in the center of so much public chaos. The day Levi and Coates recorded &#x201C;Remain Calm,&#x201D; Levi arrived with two CD turntables and thumb drives full of samples and premade digital files &#x2014; all of which, in one way or another, had reminded her of Coates. She was thinking of music not in terms of classical or hip-hop or any other genre, but in terms of people. Some music was Oliver Coates music. Some music was Mica Levi music.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Listening to &#x201C;Barok Main,&#x201D; one of the longest tracks on &#x201C;Remain Calm,&#x201D; Coates and Levi agreed that it was also probably the most unified piece. &#x201C;This was a nice peaceful moment, just after lunch,&#x201D; Coates said, recalling the rainy day they recorded it.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Levi said: &#x201C;I was thoughtful of what could fit here. Something that wasn&#x2019;t too thick, too full of information.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">They listened a bit longer.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">The track swelled and echoed, and it was hard to know where Coates&#x2019;s cello stopped and Levi&#x2019;s sound began. &#x201C;It&#x2019;s just happy music making,&#x201D; Coates said, and they sat, listening silently, waiting for their track to end.&#x2666;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><em>Ryan Bradley is a writer based in Los Angeles.</em></p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n          <div class=\"read-more\">Read more</div>\n        </div>\n      </section>\n      \n    \n      <section id=\"/ka-mourn-at-night\" data-id=\"ka-mourn-at-night\"\n        class=\"m-section feature ka\">\n        \n        <header class=\"m-header\">\n          <div class=\"m-header-meta\">\n            <h2 class=\"m-number\">12<span class=\"dot\">.<span></h2>\n            \n            <h2 class=\"m-headline\">\n              <span class=\"m-player\" data-slug=\"ka-mourn-at-night\">\n                <audio class=\"m-audio\" src=\"https://p.scdn.co/mp3-preview/000d19c1068bd25c2a49af10062dbbcf02e0b8e5?cid=null\"></audio>\n              </span>\n              <span class=\"m-headline-text\">Mourn at Night</span>\n            </h2>\n            \n            <div class=\"m-header-footer\">\n              <p class=\"m-leadin\">Putting out fires by day, music by night.</p>\n              <p class=\"m-byline\">By JODY ROSEN</p>\n            </div>\n            <div class=\"tools-wrapper\">\n              <div class=\"m-sharetools\" data-shares=\"facebook,twitter\"\n                data-url=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2017/03/09/magazine/25-songs-that-tell-us-where-music-is-going.html#/ka-mourn-at-night\"\n                data-title=\"What Ka's work can tell us about where music is headed.\"\n                data-description=\"\"></div>\n              \n              <a class=\"m-full-track-link\" href=\"https://open.spotify.com/track/6zao3czBFKk1zbmIe3azJd\" target=\"_blank\">\n                <img class=\"spotify-icon\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2017/03/03/music2017/assets/icons/spotify.svg\" />Full Track\n              </a>\n              \n            </div>\n            \n              <h4 class=\"m-artist\">Ka</h4>\n            \n          </div>\n        </header>\n        \n        <div class=\"content \">\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><span class=\"dropcap\">I</span>f you buy a record on brownsvilleka.com, the website of the rapper Ka, it is Ka himself who packs up your purchase and puts it in the mail. Every few days, Ka sits in a study in his home near Prospect Park in Brooklyn and goes through the orders on his site. He was there on a morning not long ago, with a MacBook propped on his knees. &#x201C;This guy, from California, he bought the whole discography,&#x201D; Ka said. On the floor were cardboard boxes holding copies of his five full-length albums. He placed five CDs in a padded envelope. &#x201C;I&#x2019;ll try to get those out tomorrow,&#x201D; he said. A message on the website warns: Please have patience with your order, I don&#x2019;t go to the post office every day.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">There was a time when Ka took a guerrilla approach to promoting his music. In 2007, he had 1,000 CDs made of his debut album, &#x201C;Iron Works.&#x201D; &#x201C;I gave them to my cousins, my friends. I still had, like, 990 CDs left. So I started giving them away. I&#x2019;d drive around the city, and if I heard music coming from the next car at a red light &#x2014; boom-boom-boom &#x2014; I&#x2019;d say, &#x2018;You like hip-hop?&#x2019; Then I&#x2019;d toss a CD in their window.&#x201D; By 2012, when Ka released his second album, &#x201C;Grief Pedigree,&#x201D; he had a sizable-enough following to make an announcement on Twitter and hold a curbside sale in Greenwich Village. This has become a tradition: On the day that Ka drops a new album, he tweets, turns up on a street corner and sells a few dozen records out of the trunk of his car.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            \n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  <figure\n  id=\"media-100000004967579\"\n  class=\"media photo vertical jumbo\">\n  <div class=\"image\">\n    <img src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music1-KA/12music1-KA-master180-v2.jpg\"\n    data-ratio=\"1.5\"\n    data-pattern=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music1-KA/12music1-KA-{{size}}-v2.jpg\"\n    data-widths='[{\"slug\":\"blogSmallInline\",\"size\":151},{\"slug\":\"master180\",\"size\":180},{\"slug\":\"blog225\",\"size\":225},{\"slug\":\"slide\",\"size\":334},{\"slug\":\"popup\",\"size\":334},{\"slug\":\"tmagSF\",\"size\":362},{\"slug\":\"blog427\",\"size\":427},{\"slug\":\"blog480\",\"size\":480},{\"slug\":\"master495\",\"size\":495},{\"slug\":\"blog533\",\"size\":533},{\"slug\":\"tmagArticle\",\"size\":592},{\"slug\":\"articleLarge\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"master675\",\"size\":675},{\"slug\":\"jumbo\",\"size\":683},{\"slug\":\"master1050\",\"size\":1050},{\"slug\":\"superJumbo\",\"size\":1366}]'\n    itemprop=\"url\"/>\n  </div>\n  <figcaption class=\"caption\">\n    <span class=\"caption-text\"></span>\n    <span class=\"credit\">Erik Madigan Heck for The New York Times</span>\n  </figcaption>\n</figure>\n\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">When music writers use the word &#x201C;indie,&#x201D; they generally have a certain kind of recording artist in mind: a white bohemian, with a battered guitar and a tricky haircut, who dwells on the distant outskirts of the pop-industrial complex. Ka, whose given name is Kaseem Ryan, doesn&#x2019;t quite fit that description, but he may be the platonic ideal of an independent musician in 2017. It would be hard to find a more thoroughgoing D.I.Y. musical enterprise. Ka is the rare rapper who handles both rhymes and beats, writing his lyrics and producing the music that accompanies them. He has directed most of his videos, and he self-releases his music, on his own label. It is not a profitable venture. Ka spends as much as $15,000 per album and has never broken even. &#x201C;I make music, but I&#x2019;m not in the music business,&#x201D; he says. &#x201C;It&#x2019;s my hobby.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">&#x201C;It&#x2019;s my hobby&#x201D; is one of Ka&#x2019;s mantras. It&#x2019;s accurate, but also an understatement. Over the past several years, Ka has released some of the most gripping music in any genre. His records offer a poignant, distinctive take on classic New York hip-hop: vivid stories of street life and struggle narrated in virtuosic rhymes over music of bleak beauty. His output has won him a small but passionate fan base and critical raves in Pitchfork and Spin. The magazine The Fader, an influential arbiter of musical cool, <a href=\"http://www.thefader.com/2016/10/04/ka-interview-new-york-post\">proclaimed Ka</a> &#x201C;New York rap&#x2019;s greatest living treasure.&#x201D; Praise has also come from well-known musicians. In 2015, the Los Angeles M.C. Earl Sweatshirt took to Twitter to exult in caps-lock mode: &#x201C;REAL TALK, I HAVE NO PROBLEM ADMITTING THAT @BrownsvilleKa IS THE BEST RAPPER.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">For Ka to have won even modest recognition is an improbable underdog triumph. He spent much of the 1990s trying to make it as a rapper, quit music altogether and returned a decade later, releasing his solo debut at age 35. Today he is 44. This career trajectory defies one of the seemingly immutable laws of pop, and of hip-hop in particular, a genre in which the cult of youth and novelty is especially pronounced.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Then there is the matter of Ka&#x2019;s day job. He is a captain in the New York City Fire Department. It&#x2019;s a story that has the ring of folklore: One of the great recording artists of the current decade is a musical moonlighter, a middle-aged man who earns his living as the decorated company commander of Engine 235 in Bedford-Stuyvesant. &#x201C;I try to keep my job and music separate,&#x201D; Ka says. &#x201C;I never wanted be &#x2018;The Rapping Captain.&#x2019; I try to be a good firefighter. And when I come home, I try to make some dope music.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Last Aug. 21, Ka&#x2019;s photo appeared on the cover of <a href=\"http://nypost.com/2016/08/21/fdny-veteran-bad-mouths-cops-in-double-life-as-rapper/\">The New York Post</a> under a lurid headline: &#x201C;FLAME THROWER: FDNY Captain moonlights as anti-cop rapper.&#x201D; The article purported to be an expos&#xE9;, but the facts of what The Post termed Ka&#x2019;s &#x201C;double life&#x201D; have long been known to his Fire Department colleagues and to readers of the music press. Ka put it succinctly: &#x201C;The Post don&#x2019;t like rap. They don&#x2019;t like firemen. And they don&#x2019;t like black people. With me, they had all three.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">In fact, Ka&#x2019;s music bears little resemblance to the &#x201C;gangsta rap&#x201D; caricature of tabloid fulminations. In Ka&#x2019;s songs, crime and violence are omnipresent but deglamorized. It&#x2019;s here that Ka&#x2019;s age shows: He raps in the voice of a world-weary O.G., looking back at &#x201C;the hustle&#x201D; with regret and dismay, and reckoning with its spiritual toll. Ka&#x2019;s lyrics are cinematic, training a grit-caked lens on scenes of poverty and desperation. In &#x201C;No Downtime&#x201D; (2012), he raps: &#x201C;I admit, not from environment that let a child flourish/In the street, slim physique, wild courage/Five deep, trying to eat, looking malnourished.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            \n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  <figure\n  id=\"media-100000004967600\"\n  class=\"media photo horizontal jumbo\">\n  <div class=\"image\">\n    <img src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music2-KA/12music2-KA-master180-v2.jpg\"\n    data-ratio=\"0.6666666666666666\"\n    data-pattern=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music2-KA/12music2-KA-{{size}}-v2.jpg\"\n    data-widths='[{\"slug\":\"blogSmallInline\",\"size\":151},{\"slug\":\"master180\",\"size\":180},{\"slug\":\"blog225\",\"size\":225},{\"slug\":\"tmagSF\",\"size\":362},{\"slug\":\"blog427\",\"size\":427},{\"slug\":\"blog480\",\"size\":480},{\"slug\":\"master495\",\"size\":495},{\"slug\":\"blog533\",\"size\":533},{\"slug\":\"tmagArticle\",\"size\":592},{\"slug\":\"articleLarge\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"slide\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"popup\",\"size\":650},{\"slug\":\"master675\",\"size\":675},{\"slug\":\"jumbo\",\"size\":1024},{\"slug\":\"master1050\",\"size\":1050},{\"slug\":\"superJumbo\",\"size\":2048}]'\n    itemprop=\"url\"/>\n  </div>\n  <figcaption class=\"caption\">\n    <span class=\"caption-text\">Ka in Brooklyn, where he is company commander of Engine 235.</span>\n    <span class=\"credit\">Erik Madigan Heck for The New York Times</span>\n  </figcaption>\n</figure>\n\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">These stories aren&#x2019;t fictional. Ka grew up poor, in Brownsville. As a teenager, he drifted into the drug trade, dealing crack and selling firearms. It was a &#x201C;scary and lonely&#x201D; existence, Ka says, one that left a legacy of trauma and a wealth of material, a burden that&#x2019;s also a muse. &#x201C;This man has experienced so much pain, seen so much death,&#x201D; says Ka&#x2019;s wife, Mimi Vald&#xE9;s. &#x201C;It&#x2019;s all over his music.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">If Ka is not in the music business, his wife definitively is. Vald&#xE9;s is the former editor in chief of Vibe, the music magazine. Today she is chief creative officer for i am OTHER, a multimedia company founded by Pharrell Williams, the superstar rapper-singer-producer. Vald&#xE9;s&#x2019;s professional circle includes some of the world&#x2019;s most famous musicians. It is conceivable that Ka could trade on his wife&#x2019;s connections to raise his profile. But a commercial breakthrough is far-fetched, and a prospect for which Ka seems constitutionally ill equipped. He has performed just a few live shows and professes little interest in playing more. He&#x2019;s a homebody who prefers to sit in a closet-size work space and make records.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Those records are, in the best sense, strange. Critics often call Ka a revivalist of rap&#x2019;s &#x2019;90s golden age. But he is by no means &#x201C;retro.&#x201D; He takes the rudiments of &#x2019;90s rap and fashions them into an eccentric other thing. His albums have idiosyncratic conceptual frames: &#x201C;The Night&#x2019;s Gambit&#x201D; (2013) is full of chess imagery; &#x201C;Days With Dr. Yen Lo&#x201D; (2015), a collaboration with the producer Preservation, is an elaborate gloss on the novel &#x201C;The Manchurian Candidate.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">But it is the sound, the vibe, that sets Ka&#x2019;s music apart. His songs are unnervingly quiet and still; they hold a listener in thrall because they hold so much back. Ka&#x2019;s beats do not boom, they slither and slink and creep. Often the songs discard drums altogether, opening vast spaces that are filled by samples in brooding minor keys. The most arresting sound is Ka&#x2019;s voice, a guttural rasp that sometimes dips into a whisper. It is an unshakable voice of experience, delivering hard-boiled tales and hard-won wisdom.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            \n            <figure\n  id=\"graphic-100000004976467\"\n  class=\"media graphic size-300\"\n  data-minimum-width=\"300\" >\n  <div class=\"interactive interactive-embedded\"\n       style=\"max-width: 300px\">\n    \n    <figcaption class=\"interactive-caption\">\n      <h2 class=\"interactive-headline\"></h2>\n      <h4 class=\"interactive-summary\"></h4>\n    </figcaption>\n    \n    <div class=\"interactive-graphic\"><script>function getFlexData() { return {\"data\":{\"quote\":\"A legacy of trauma, a burden that\u2019s also a muse.\",\"attribution\":\"\"}}; }var NYTD=NYTD || {}; NYTD.FlexTypes = NYTD.FlexTypes || []; NYTD.FlexTypes.push({\"target\":\"FT100000004976467\",\"type\":\"Pull Quote\",\"data\":{\"quote\":\"A legacy of trauma, a burden that\u2019s also a muse.\",\"attribution\":\"\"}});</script><link href=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2015/02/10/mag-pullquote-redesign/assets/style.css\" type=\"text/css\" rel=\"stylesheet\">\r\n\r\n<script>\r\n  var NYTG_ASSETS = \"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2015/02/10/mag-pullquote-redesign/b585ac490ed3103c57423bc4f581ffac30ebc5df/\",\r\n      NYTG_BIG_ASSETS = \"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2015/02/10/mag-pullquote-redesign/assets/\",\r\n      _gaq = _gaq || [];\r\n  require(['foundation/main'], function() {\r\n    require([\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2015/02/10/mag-pullquote-redesign/assets/build.js\"]); // generated from src/script.js\r\n  });\r\n</script><div id=\"FT100000004976467\"></div></div>\n    <div class=\"footer\">\n      <div class=\"interactive-notes\"></div>\n      <div class=\"interactive-source\"></div>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</figure>\n\n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">&#x201C;Mourn at Night,&#x201D; from the 2016 album &#x201C;Honor Killed the Samurai,&#x201D; is a typical Ka mood piece &#x2014; a rap-noir, burnished to lustrous dark hue. The song&#x2019;s title establishes the terms: The mood is elegiac, the setting nocturnal. &#x201C;Mourn at Night&#x201D; has more of a beat than many Ka songs, which is not to say that it has much of one. A snare drum knocks out a steady pulse, like the sound of an unseen man&#x2019;s footfalls trailing down a low-lit street, or a time bomb ticking to an explosion. The music is nudged forward by guitar and keyboard arpeggios, which chime and toll behind a somber refrain: &#x201C;He gone/We mourn at night.&#x201D; In the final verse, the action erupts: Gunfire blasts out of the shadows, strafing a street corner crowded with young drug dealers.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <blockquote class=\"paragraph\">\n<p>On the block with the rock</p><p>Up nights, dribbling</p><p>Time spent getting bent</p><p>Hard to be upright citizen</p><p>It&#x2019;s more than troubling</p><p>Same corners are bubbling</p><p>The pawns are huddling</p><p>Then all of a sudden it&#x2019;s</p><p><em>Pop, pop, pop</em></p></blockquote>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Ka excels at this kind of writing, brisk storytelling that unfolds in a pileup of rhymes and puns. What you won&#x2019;t find much of on his records is the meat-and-potatoes of rap: trash talk. &#x201C;What do I have to brag about?&#x201D; Ka says. &#x201C;I ain&#x2019;t winning. I&#x2019;m a working man, with a blue-collar job. I&#x2019;m running into burning buildings for a living. So I speak about the things that I did, the things I pray I never have to do again. How do I finish my life in grace? That&#x2019;s what I rap about.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">On those occasions when Ka does slip in a mild boast, it&#x2019;s about his skills as an M.C. In &#x201C;Mourn at Night&#x201D; he permits himself one such moment, the testimony of a bootstrapper who has survived untold trials to ply his trade &#x2014; as he would have it, his hobby &#x2014; in the soundproof booth of a recording studio. &#x201C;We was born in the thorns, few arose,&#x201D; Ka raps. &#x201C;Once in town&#x2019;s noose/Now in soundproofs/Pursuing goals.&#x201D;&#x2666;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><em>Jody Rosen is a contributing writer for the magazine</em>.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n          <div class=\"read-more\">Read more</div>\n        </div>\n      </section>\n      \n      <div class=\"m-ad-container\"></div>\n      \n    \n      <section id=\"/shirley-caesar-hold-my-mule\" data-id=\"shirley-caesar-hold-my-mule\"\n        class=\"m-section  shirley-caesar\">\n        \n        <header class=\"m-header\">\n          <div class=\"m-header-meta\">\n            <h2 class=\"m-number\">13<span class=\"dot\">.<span></h2>\n            \n            <h2 class=\"m-headline\">\n              <span class=\"m-player\" data-slug=\"shirley-caesar-hold-my-mule\">\n                <audio class=\"m-audio\" src=\"https://p.scdn.co/mp3-preview/269aa3ff7c12c4b47a69259d247d5e9e4f222590?cid=null\"></audio>\n              </span>\n              <span class=\"m-headline-text\">Hold My Mule</span>\n            </h2>\n            <h4 class=\"m-artist\">Shirley Caesar</h4>\n            \n            <div class=\"m-header-footer\">\n              <p class=\"m-leadin\">When gospel goes viral &#x2014; with an abundance of pure joy.</p>\n              <p class=\"m-byline\">By GREG HOWARD</p>\n            </div>\n            <div class=\"tools-wrapper\">\n              <div class=\"m-sharetools\" data-shares=\"facebook,twitter\"\n                data-url=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2017/03/09/magazine/25-songs-that-tell-us-where-music-is-going.html#/shirley-caesar-hold-my-mule\"\n                data-title=\"What Shirley Caesar's work can tell us about where music is headed.\"\n                data-description=\"\"></div>\n              \n              <a class=\"m-full-track-link\" href=\"https://open.spotify.com/track/0JjsI172ped2hF5v27o28J\" target=\"_blank\">\n                <img class=\"spotify-icon\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2017/03/03/music2017/assets/icons/spotify.svg\" />Full Track\n              </a>\n              \n            </div>\n            \n          </div>\n        </header>\n        \n        <div class=\"content \">\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">It was 29 years ago that the legendary gospel artist Shirley Caesar first recorded the song &#x201C;Hold My Mule.&#x201D; It was a banger then, and it still is. She has performed it many times, and at least once, 10 years ago, someone filmed her in a church. About midway through the nine-and-a-half-minute video, the band and the organ, which riff all the way through, fall quiet. Caesar hunches over, gazes into the distance and, playing the role of the song&#x2019;s protagonist, Shouting John &#x2014; a farmer who has been reprimanded by the deacons at his local church for celebrating too much, dancing too wildly, speaking too loudly (and sometimes in tongues) &#x2014; enumerates the many blessings God has bestowed on him and his farm. She counts each blessing off on her fingers and raps: &#x201C;I&#x2019;ve got beans, greens, potatoes, tomatoes, lambs, rams, hogs, dogs, chickens, turkeys, rabbits&#x201D; &#x2014; and then she shouts, &#x201C;you name it!&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">The band kicks in again, and a slew of sonic histrionics, pyrotechnics and acrobatics follows. But that list is the climax, the moment when Caesar basks in the warmth of God&#x2019;s grace, when she and you both feel closest to him.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">This was the moment that stood out to a musician called DJ Suede the Remix God, who just before Thanksgiving took that snippet &#x2014; just eight seconds in all &#x2014; and <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=UR8p9z8AK0Q\">laid it over a trap-style hip-hop</a> beat of his own making. The story of Shouting John and his fussy deacons disappeared, leaving behind nothing but that list of blessings and Caesar&#x2019;s euphoric &#x201C;you name it!&#x201D; completely stripped of context.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            \n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  <figure\n  id=\"media-100000004968876\"\n  class=\"media photo horizontal jumbo\">\n  <div class=\"image\">\n    <img src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music16-caesar/12mag-12caesar-t_CA0-master180.jpg\"\n    data-ratio=\"0.9388888888888889\"\n    data-pattern=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music16-caesar/12mag-12caesar-t_CA0-{{size}}.jpg\"\n    data-widths='[{\"slug\":\"blogSmallInline\",\"size\":151},{\"slug\":\"master180\",\"size\":180},{\"slug\":\"blog225\",\"size\":225},{\"slug\":\"tmagSF\",\"size\":362},{\"slug\":\"blog427\",\"size\":427},{\"slug\":\"blog480\",\"size\":480},{\"slug\":\"master495\",\"size\":495},{\"slug\":\"slide\",\"size\":532},{\"slug\":\"popup\",\"size\":532},{\"slug\":\"blog533\",\"size\":533},{\"slug\":\"tmagArticle\",\"size\":592},{\"slug\":\"articleLarge\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"master675\",\"size\":675},{\"slug\":\"jumbo\",\"size\":1024},{\"slug\":\"master1050\",\"size\":1050},{\"slug\":\"superJumbo\",\"size\":2048}]'\n    itemprop=\"url\"/>\n  </div>\n  <figcaption class=\"caption\">\n    <span class=\"caption-text\"></span>\n    <span class=\"credit\">Photo illustration by Trey Wright. Caesar: Michael Ochs Archives/Getty Images.</span>\n  </figcaption>\n</figure>\n\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Suede then offered the beat to the internet, calling it the U Name It Challenge and inviting others to put their own spins on it. The singer Chris Brown recorded <a href=\"https://youtu.be/FKBnETEiRVk\">a video</a> dancing to it. Countless other dancers and rappers followed him. The challenge went megaviral. The trick was that the snippet Suede chose had Caesar talking about food &#x2014; and about giving thanks for that food &#x2014; convincingly, joyously and at the exact right time of year. Her ecstatic cry made it universal.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">The best came a few days before Thanksgiving, when an Atlanta-based rapper named Grey responded to Suede&#x2019;s challenge with <a href=\"https://www.instagram.com/p/BMzFwj9jQyP/\">a verse of his own</a>. Grey is a vegan, unlike Shouting John, but a quick jaunt through his social media identifies him as every bit the evangelist of his philosophy that Caesar is for Christ. From inside a parked car, Grey unleashed a verse describing the Thanksgiving feast he was preparing for his friends and family: &#x201C;Jump into the whip and hit Whole Foods early/Get everything to get that soul food working.&#x201D; There would be vegan cornbread, vegan stuffing, &#x201C;greens with no ham in it,&#x201D; pies whipped up with almond milk, &#x201C;mac and cheese to collard greens to black-eyed peas/Get a roast of that tofu turkey.&#x201D; Then the sample of Caesar&#x2019;s list, with a little amendment from Grey: He endorsed the &#x201C;beans, greens, potatoes, tomatoes&#x201D; part, then noted that he wasn&#x2019;t messing with the &#x201C;lambs, rams, hogs, dogs.&#x201D; It&#x2019;s almost certainly the best and funniest verse of his life.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">While taking a gospel sample and warping it into a trap song not about Christ but about the wonders of soy and fake meat scrubs Caesar&#x2019;s joy of its context, the joy embedded in her voice still translates, is still familiar. I&#x2019;m not religious. But I grew up going to church, dancing and singing to raucous gospel bands and choirs nearly every Sunday. Once, after a particularly rousing concert, I walked from my seat to the front of the auditorium to be baptized and join the church, only to come to my senses once I got to the altar. I never truly believed in Caesar&#x2019;s God, but I was swept away as so many nonbelievers can be, in flashes of euphoria ignited by family and tofu and trap and everything else that sustains us.&#x2666;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><em>Greg Howard is a David Carr fellow for The New York Times.</em></p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n          <div class=\"read-more\">Read more</div>\n        </div>\n      </section>\n      \n    \n      <section id=\"/rufus-wainwright-a-womans-face-reprise-sonnet-20\" data-id=\"rufus-wainwright-a-womans-face-reprise-sonnet-20\"\n        class=\"m-section  rufus-wainwright\">\n        \n        <header class=\"m-header\">\n          <div class=\"m-header-meta\">\n            <h2 class=\"m-number\">14<span class=\"dot\">.<span></h2>\n            \n            <h2 class=\"m-headline\">\n              <span class=\"m-player\" data-slug=\"rufus-wainwright-a-womans-face-reprise-sonnet-20\">\n                <audio class=\"m-audio\" src=\"https://p.scdn.co/mp3-preview/c4735c5bc35074d5983f8ac35e70bab1fdc5a476?cid=null\"></audio>\n              </span>\n              <span class=\"m-headline-text\">A Woman\u2019s Face \u2014 Reprise (Sonnet 20)</span>\n            </h2>\n            <h4 class=\"m-artist\">Rufus Wainwright</h4>\n            \n            <div class=\"m-header-footer\">\n              <p class=\"m-leadin\">He soothes the anxieties of life as a gay man.</p>\n              <p class=\"m-byline\">By ALEXANDER CHEE</p>\n            </div>\n            <div class=\"tools-wrapper\">\n              <div class=\"m-sharetools\" data-shares=\"facebook,twitter\"\n                data-url=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2017/03/09/magazine/25-songs-that-tell-us-where-music-is-going.html#/rufus-wainwright-a-womans-face-reprise-sonnet-20\"\n                data-title=\"What Rufus Wainwright's work can tell us about where music is headed.\"\n                data-description=\"\"></div>\n              \n              <a class=\"m-full-track-link\" href=\"https://open.spotify.com/track/5D12h7kioqQdCKzoDiUfvJ\" target=\"_blank\">\n                <img class=\"spotify-icon\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2017/03/03/music2017/assets/icons/spotify.svg\" />Full Track\n              </a>\n              \n            </div>\n            \n          </div>\n        </header>\n        \n        <div class=\"content \">\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">For more than a year now, I have listened to little else in my car other than the albums of Rufus Wainwright. This obsession began when my husband and I bought a car for weekend trips: a 2009 AWD Subaru hatchback with what in retrospect seems like an ancient playback machine, a 5-disc CD player. We pulled all our old CD wallets out to the car, loaded the changer and set off for our first drive. And what had been my self-imposed exile from music came to an end that day.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Sometime between 2009 and 2015, without ever noticing it was happening, I stopped listening to music regularly. I was alienated, in part, by the new ways of listening: I don&#x2019;t like streaming music, or &#x201C;social&#x201D; music platforms, my private whims made public. Around the same time, my doctor told me I had mild depression, which would respond to exercise and a change in habits. But this mild depression did not feel mild. I felt trapped at the bottom of a swimming pool, immobilized. Everything I had to do, everything I needed to take care of, was up at the surface, and the soundtrack to this situation was silence.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">During one of our first drives, we loaded the changer solely with Rufus Wainwright albums, and I still haven&#x2019;t changed those CDs out. I was always a fan of Wainwright&#x2019;s, but I&#x2019;m something more now: His lighthearted way of penetrating my anxieties about gay life became essential. In my year of carbound listening, I played through his songbook over and over. I gained a new appreciation for his extraordinary voice, and the way its nasal timbre humanizes him, as if someone ordinary had been given extraordinary powers, midnote. I am often tricked into trying to sing along, but I&#x2019;m always outclassed. I never care. Wainwright is a storyteller, and his albums work on my imagination the same way short-story collections do &#x2014; poetically, dramatically. Singing along with his secrets became like telling mine to myself, and somehow, this helped me up from the bottom of that pool.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">A change of habits, then.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            \n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  <figure\n  id=\"media-100000004975607\"\n  class=\"media photo vertical large\">\n  <div class=\"image\">\n    <img src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music-rufuswainwright/12music-wainwright-master180.jpg\"\n    data-ratio=\"1.461111111111111\"\n    data-pattern=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music-rufuswainwright/12music-wainwright-{{size}}.jpg\"\n    data-widths='[{\"slug\":\"blogSmallInline\",\"size\":151},{\"slug\":\"master180\",\"size\":180},{\"slug\":\"blog225\",\"size\":225},{\"slug\":\"slide\",\"size\":343},{\"slug\":\"popup\",\"size\":343},{\"slug\":\"tmagSF\",\"size\":362},{\"slug\":\"blog427\",\"size\":427},{\"slug\":\"blog480\",\"size\":480},{\"slug\":\"master495\",\"size\":495},{\"slug\":\"blog533\",\"size\":533},{\"slug\":\"tmagArticle\",\"size\":592},{\"slug\":\"articleLarge\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"master675\",\"size\":675},{\"slug\":\"jumbo\",\"size\":701},{\"slug\":\"master1050\",\"size\":1050},{\"slug\":\"superJumbo\",\"size\":1402}]'\n    itemprop=\"url\"/>\n  </div>\n  <figcaption class=\"caption\">\n    <span class=\"caption-text\"></span>\n    <span class=\"credit\">Illustration by Tyler Spangler. Wainwright: Noam Galai/WireImage.</span>\n  </figcaption>\n</figure>\n\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Joan Didion has written of how the ability to make a note &#x201C;when something came to mind&#x201D; was the &#x201C;difference between being able to write and not being able to write&#x201D; &#x2014; a sign of life. For me, looking for new music from favorite musicians is also a sign of life. This fall, I finally thought to look for a new Wainwright album. What I found was &#x201C;Take All My Loves,&#x201D; released early last year. Wainwright adapts nine Shakespearean sonnets into a kind of variety show, with William Shatner; Helena Bonham Carter; Carrie Fisher; Florence Welch; the soprano Anna Prohaska; his sister, Martha Wainwright; the BBC Orchestra; and the Berlin String Section. He is on the cover in full Queen Elizabeth I drag, flowers in his hair. My favorite track is &#x201C;A Woman&#x2019;s Face &#x2014; Reprise (Sonnet 20),&#x201D; which Wainwright first interpreted in 2010 on &#x201C;All Days Are Nights: Songs for Lulu.&#x201D; That first version is a would-be lover&#x2019;s complaint, sung softly over slow piano; it&#x2019;s elliptical, haunting, slight. A new version on &#x201C;Take All My Loves&#x201D; is almost a torch song &#x2014; teasing, raunchy, openly seductive, as if Wainwright were seated on a piano in a slinky gown and Elizabethan collar, all made up and beckoning.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">The poem is, famously, the locus of academic debates over Shakespeare&#x2019;s queerness. His misogyny too. The sonnet is about an impossible love for a young man who the poet claims has all the beauty of a woman, but for the &#x201C;one thing.&#x201D; I had read it, but I never really understood it the way I did when I heard Wainwright sing it: It&#x2019;s a refrain many gay men know from waiting out the passion of a man who doesn&#x2019;t &#x201C;think of himself that way,&#x201D; despite his feelings for you. &#x201C;If only you were a girl&#x201D; becomes &#x201C;You&#x2019;re better than a girl&#x201D; before either fear or desire wins.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Wainwright knows this territory well. His second album, &#x201C;Poses,&#x201D; was dedicated to one of these reluctant, not-quite-queer lovers, stories from when Wainwright was the kind of seductive youth the sonnet describes. Now, for the duration of this song, he is the reluctant lover.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">As a storyteller, Wainwright has always been more of a memoirist than a novelist. He made his reputation singing wise songs of impossible loves and rejection, turning personal pain into public art. Wainwright is now married to J&#xF6;rn Weisbrodt, an arts impresario, and he has a daughter with his friend Lorca Cohen, a photographer and the daughter of Leonard Cohen. Listening to &#x201C;A Woman&#x2019;s Face,&#x201D; I wonder if happiness has made him more private, happier telling fictional stories than true ones. His next project is an opera about the Roman emperor Hadrian, who, out of grief at the death of his lover Antinous, created a religion around him.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">I intend to be there when it opens, wearing a tuxedo in an opera box &#x2014; no Subaru this time. I&#x2019;ll try not to sing along.&#x2666;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><em>Alexander Chee is the author of the novels &#x201C;Edinburgh&#x201D; and &#x201C;The Queen of the Night.&#x201D;</em></p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n          <div class=\"read-more\">Read more</div>\n        </div>\n      </section>\n      \n      <div class=\"m-ad-container\"></div>\n      \n    \n      <section id=\"/james-mcmurtry-copper-canteen\" data-id=\"james-mcmurtry-copper-canteen\"\n        class=\"m-section feature james-mcmurtry\">\n        \n        <header class=\"m-header\">\n          <div class=\"m-header-meta\">\n            <h2 class=\"m-number\">15<span class=\"dot\">.<span></h2>\n            \n            <h2 class=\"m-headline\">\n              <span class=\"m-player\" data-slug=\"james-mcmurtry-copper-canteen\">\n                <audio class=\"m-audio\" src=\"https://p.scdn.co/mp3-preview/753906750a2306ba2f90ed31fdbdb69152805256?cid=null\"></audio>\n              </span>\n              <span class=\"m-headline-text\">Copper Canteen</span>\n            </h2>\n            \n            <div class=\"m-header-footer\">\n              <p class=\"m-leadin\">The danger of being a singer-songwriter.</p>\n              <p class=\"m-byline\">By RUTH GRAHAM</p>\n            </div>\n            <div class=\"tools-wrapper\">\n              <div class=\"m-sharetools\" data-shares=\"facebook,twitter\"\n                data-url=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2017/03/09/magazine/25-songs-that-tell-us-where-music-is-going.html#/james-mcmurtry-copper-canteen\"\n                data-title=\"What James McMurtry's work can tell us about where music is headed.\"\n                data-description=\"\"></div>\n              \n              <a class=\"m-full-track-link\" href=\"https://open.spotify.com/track/2jG8qg7KDYWKm9smYCXz1i\" target=\"_blank\">\n                <img class=\"spotify-icon\"
