src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2017/03/03/music2017/assets/icons/spotify.svg\" />Full Track\n              </a>\n              \n            </div>\n            \n              <h4 class=\"m-artist\">James McMurtry</h4>\n            \n          </div>\n        </header>\n        \n        <div class=\"content \">\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><span class=\"dropcap\">F</span>ifteen minutes after finishing an acoustic concert one evening in January, the Texas singer-songwriter James McMurtry was backstage talking about his great-grandfather. The man had lost his own father and grandfather to post-Civil War skirmishes in Missouri, McMurtry said, so he and his wife fled the state. They settled in Denton County, Tex., 40-some miles northwest of the renovated Art Deco theater in Dallas that their great-grandson headlined that night. &#x201C;They had to time it just right, because if you went West at that time, you could get into more violence because the Comanche were still active,&#x201D; he said. &#x201C;They farmed there for five or six years, waiting to make sure the Comanche weren&#x2019;t coming back.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">The story was a good example of how forces like politics and war can affect regular people&#x2019;s lives, which is a running theme in McMurtry&#x2019;s work. McMurtry, who has released 12 albums over a 28-year career, has a reputation in some quarters as a political songwriter, in part because one of his most popular songs is an angry-lefty anthem. That song, &#x201C;We Can&#x2019;t Make It Here Anymore,&#x201D; laments that minimum wage &#x201C;won&#x2019;t pay for a roof, won&#x2019;t pay for a drink,&#x201D; and that the children of the poor are the ones who end up fighting in rich men&#x2019;s wars. Released shortly before the 2004 election, the song swept through an America hollowed out by departed manufacturing jobs and the middle-class stability that went with them. A few years after its release, the critic Robert Christgau named it the best song of the decade.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">But McMurtry more often writes about how seemingly distant political concerns nudge his characters&#x2019; choices and prod at their psyches: the stretched budget of the Veterans Affairs Department, or the birth of a new national park&#x2019;s consuming the neighbors&#x2019; land through eminent domain. In &#x201C;Sixty Acres,&#x201D; the narrator laments that when his grandmother died, he inherited a plot of unpromising farmland while his cousin got &#x201C;the good land,&#x201D; zoned commercial: &#x201C;Looks like a Walmart waiting to happen/I mean to tell you it&#x2019;s a pot of gold.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            \n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  <figure\n  id=\"media-100000004967597\"\n  class=\"media photo vertical jumbo\">\n  <div class=\"image\">\n    <img src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music7-MCMURTRY/12music7-MCMURTRY-master180-v2.jpg\"\n    data-ratio=\"1.2277777777777779\"\n    data-pattern=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music7-MCMURTRY/12music7-MCMURTRY-{{size}}-v2.jpg\"\n    data-widths='[{\"slug\":\"blogSmallInline\",\"size\":151},{\"slug\":\"master180\",\"size\":180},{\"slug\":\"blog225\",\"size\":225},{\"slug\":\"tmagSF\",\"size\":362},{\"slug\":\"slide\",\"size\":407},{\"slug\":\"popup\",\"size\":407},{\"slug\":\"blog427\",\"size\":427},{\"slug\":\"blog480\",\"size\":480},{\"slug\":\"master495\",\"size\":495},{\"slug\":\"blog533\",\"size\":533},{\"slug\":\"tmagArticle\",\"size\":592},{\"slug\":\"articleLarge\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"master675\",\"size\":675},{\"slug\":\"jumbo\",\"size\":833},{\"slug\":\"master1050\",\"size\":1050},{\"slug\":\"superJumbo\",\"size\":1666}]'\n    itemprop=\"url\"/>\n  </div>\n  <figcaption class=\"caption\">\n    <span class=\"caption-text\"></span>\n    <span class=\"credit\">Erik Madigan Heck for The New York Times</span>\n  </figcaption>\n</figure>\n\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">McMurtry&#x2019;s father is the great Texas novelist Larry McMurtry, and his mother, Jo Scott McMurtry, is a former English professor specializing in Shakespeare. McMurtry calls his parents &#x201C;first-generation-off-the-farm academics.&#x201D; He was raised in secular urban homes and even attended boarding school for a spell. But he dropped out of the University of Arizona, and he remains fluent in his extended family&#x2019;s dialect of ranches, oil fields and Jesus Christ. He has been on tour almost constantly since the late 1980s, and he just takes note of what he sees through the windshield, he said, like banners welcoming home soldiers in small towns.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">At an upscale barbecue restaurant near his hotel in Dallas, where we met before his concert, our talk turned to tribalism and anti-intellectualism. His grandparents wanted their children to go to college to get better jobs, he said, but they didn&#x2019;t want them to become intellectuals. Later he referred to the broader middle-American &#x201C;hatred of anyone who is perceived to be getting a free ride of any kind&#x201D; and the racism woven into that ethos: &#x201C;The image of the black welfare queen driving the Cadillac while good white folk drove Oldsmobiles was the boogeyman of the &#x2019;80s. Now I suppose it&#x2019;s Mexican immigrants getting free health care.&#x201D; Over the course of an hour, he talked about the military-industrial complex, &#x201C;embedded&#x201D; war journalism and the role of charisma in electoral politics. I didn&#x2019;t know whether to believe him when he said he didn&#x2019;t read many books.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">McMurtry had ordered black coffee and a plate of fried oysters. In a few hours, he would take the stage alone with his guitar, and in a few weeks, he and his band would leave for a European tour that would carry them from Ireland to Italy, playing 33 nights in a row. McMurtry&#x2019;s music is usually classified as &#x201C;Americana&#x201D;; it&#x2019;s at turns jagged and raucous, and at others deliberative and wistful. Other singers have smoother voices. But a movie star&#x2019;s perfect face can work against his authenticity as an actor, and McMurtry&#x2019;s unassuming vocal style and stage presence bring to the foreground the voices populating his songs. &#x201C;There&#x2019;s a danger in being a singer-songwriter,&#x201D; he said. &#x201C;You&#x2019;re writing a character&#x2019;s point of view, and you&#x2019;ve got to sing it yourself. It comes out of your voice, so everybody thinks it&#x2019;s your opinion.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Although I&#x2019;ve been listening to him for years, I have begun to think of him as interpreter of the places &#x201C;out here in the middle,&#x201D; as he puts it one song. He has written about Cheyenne, Wyo.; Tulsa, Okla.; and Wewahitchka, Fla; about crab fishermen, soldiers and Walmart stockers. His songs tap into resentments about things like coastal attitudes of superiority and political correctness. His narrators are often white men who know the Bible, own guns and give their kids a nip of vodka in their Cherry Coke to get through long road trips. A Texan friend of mine likes to say that McMurtry writes as though he has spent time eavesdropping on conversations in every Dairy Queen in America. Stephen King, who owns a classic-rock station in Maine, has written that he &#x201C;may be the truest, fiercest songwriter of his generation.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">McMurtry has seen things change in rural America over the last few decades, he said, the curdling of patriotism and self-reliance into something uglier. Gun ownership, for example, has become an identity, or even like a cult. He&#x2019;s a hunter &#x2014; deer, turkey, wild hogs &#x2014; and he said gun shows and shops used to be friendly spaces. But now there&#x2019;s a suspicion in the air if &#x201C;you don&#x2019;t have the right hat or the right haircut.&#x201D; McMurtry himself has both, but he also has the kind of detachment required for skeptical reportage.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            \n            <figure\n  id=\"graphic-100000004976563\"\n  class=\"media graphic size-300\"\n  data-minimum-width=\"300\" >\n  <div class=\"interactive interactive-embedded\"\n       style=\"max-width: 300px\">\n    \n    <figcaption class=\"interactive-caption\">\n      <h2 class=\"interactive-headline\"></h2>\n      <h4 class=\"interactive-summary\"></h4>\n    </figcaption>\n    \n    <div class=\"interactive-graphic\"><script>function getFlexData() { return {\"data\":{\"quote\":\"\u2018Everybody thinks it\u2019s your opinion.\u2019\",\"attribution\":\"\"}}; }var NYTD=NYTD || {}; NYTD.FlexTypes = NYTD.FlexTypes || []; NYTD.FlexTypes.push({\"target\":\"FT100000004976563\",\"type\":\"Pull Quote\",\"data\":{\"quote\":\"\u2018Everybody thinks it\u2019s your opinion.\u2019\",\"attribution\":\"\"}});</script><link href=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2015/02/10/mag-pullquote-redesign/assets/style.css\" type=\"text/css\" rel=\"stylesheet\">\r\n\r\n<script>\r\n  var NYTG_ASSETS = \"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2015/02/10/mag-pullquote-redesign/b585ac490ed3103c57423bc4f581ffac30ebc5df/\",\r\n      NYTG_BIG_ASSETS = \"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2015/02/10/mag-pullquote-redesign/assets/\",\r\n      _gaq = _gaq || [];\r\n  require(['foundation/main'], function() {\r\n    require([\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2015/02/10/mag-pullquote-redesign/assets/build.js\"]); // generated from src/script.js\r\n  });\r\n</script><div id=\"FT100000004976563\"></div></div>\n    <div class=\"footer\">\n      <div class=\"interactive-notes\"></div>\n      <div class=\"interactive-source\"></div>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</figure>\n\n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">&#x201C;Copper Canteen,&#x201D; the opening track on his 2015 album, &#x201C;Complicated Game,&#x201D; is the song I&#x2019;ve returned to most since Election Day. The narrator is a hunter, a fisherman and a small-business owner. He doesn&#x2019;t go to church, but his wife does. Although retirement is in sight, and he has a pension, he hasn&#x2019;t been able to save as much money as he would like, in part because the store he owns is getting squeezed by &#x201C;the big boxes out on the bypass.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">But like most of McMurtry&#x2019;s best songs, &#x201C;Copper Canteen&#x201D; eventually resolves into a portrait of a relationship. The long marriages and old love affairs he favors eschew the hot and cold dramatics of contemporary country music, a world in which you&#x2019;re either swooning over your one true love or bashing in your ex&#x2019;s windshield with a baseball bat. The &#x201C;Copper Canteen&#x201D; narrator is singing to his wife; they&#x2019;re grandparents now, reflecting back on what it meant to &#x201C;grow up hard&#x201D; in a life that has moved by in a blur: &#x201C;This life that we craved so little we saved between the grandparents&#x2019; graves and the grandchildren&#x2019;s toys.&#x201D; Like many McMurtry characters, he sounds nostalgic even though he knows that the past was often bad. The song opens with a bang, or the suggestion of one: &#x201C;Honey, don&#x2019;t you go yellin&#x2019; at me while I&#x2019;m cleaning my gun,&#x201D; he drawls. &#x201C;I&#x2019;ll wash the blood off the tailgate when deer season&#x2019;s done.&#x201D; Defying Chekhov, the gun never goes off, and the song sweetens &#x2014; barely &#x2014; as it moves along. Though that line about the gun got a big laugh when McMurtry played it in Dallas, I still don&#x2019;t know whether to hear it as a joke or a threat, and McMurtry has never been one to offer the easy comfort of a straight answer.&#x2666;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><em>Ruth Graham is a contributing writer for Slate.</em></p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n          <div class=\"read-more\">Read more</div>\n        </div>\n      </section>\n      \n    \n      <section id=\"/solange-fubu\" data-id=\"solange-fubu\"\n        class=\"m-section  solange\">\n        \n        <header class=\"m-header\">\n          <div class=\"m-header-meta\">\n            <h2 class=\"m-number\">16<span class=\"dot\">.<span></h2>\n            \n            <h2 class=\"m-headline\">\n              <span class=\"m-player\" data-slug=\"solange-fubu\">\n                <audio class=\"m-audio\" src=\"https://p.scdn.co/mp3-preview/22c708fe665417fb70b3661036592bd5ed5815bf?cid=null\"></audio>\n              </span>\n              <span class=\"m-headline-text\">F.U.B.U.</span>\n            </h2>\n            <h4 class=\"m-artist\">Solange</h4>\n            \n            <div class=\"m-header-footer\">\n              <p class=\"m-leadin\">How a song can make its audience feel seen.</p>\n              <p class=\"m-byline\">By ANGELA FLOURNOY</p>\n            </div>\n            <div class=\"tools-wrapper\">\n              <div class=\"m-sharetools\" data-shares=\"facebook,twitter\"\n                data-url=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2017/03/09/magazine/25-songs-that-tell-us-where-music-is-going.html#/solange-fubu\"\n                data-title=\"What Solange's work can tell us about where music is headed.\"\n                data-description=\"\"></div>\n              \n              <a class=\"m-full-track-link\" href=\"https://open.spotify.com/track/2aR6SYRJxy2QsBxA459ATF\" target=\"_blank\">\n                <img class=\"spotify-icon\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2017/03/03/music2017/assets/icons/spotify.svg\" />Full Track\n              </a>\n              \n            </div>\n            \n          </div>\n        </header>\n        \n        <div class=\"content \">\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">A song should not shut a door in a listener&#x2019;s face. Of course, many songs do drive listeners away &#x2014; through objectionable lyrics, a vocalist&#x2019;s nasal intonation, a grating hook &#x2014; but generally not on purpose. Still rarer is a song that identifies its audience in explicit, demographic terms. But in the first line of Solange Knowles&#x2019;s song &#x201C;F.U.B.U.,&#x201D; she welcomes black listeners &#x2014; &#x201C;one for us,&#x201D; she proclaims &#x2014; and she shows white listeners the door.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Once this metaphorical point of entry is closed, an important conversation ensues. &#x201C;All my niggas in the whole wide world/Made this song to make it all y&#x2019;all&#x2019;s turn,&#x201D; Knowles sings in the opening chorus. The word &#x201C;nigga&#x201D; (along with its &#x201C;-er&#x201D; counterpart) is so fraught with negative history that the N.A.A.C.P. held a funeral for it in 2007. Not every black person can hear it in a song and feel the exultation that Knowles intends. Still, words take their own sweet time to die, and right now, &#x201C;nigga&#x201D; remains a go-to, often-affectionate phrase in many black circles. In &#x201C;F.U.B.U.&#x201D; Knowles uses it to draw a line in the sand: If you aren&#x2019;t supposed to say it, then this song isn&#x2019;t for you. &#x201C;For us,&#x201D; she sings &#x2014; &#x201C;this [expletive] is for us.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">The song&#x2019;s title references the clothing line of the same name &#x2014; F.U.B.U., For Us By Us. At its height in the late &#x2019;90s, F.U.B.U. was synonymous with black cool: I wore it in high school despite the clothes being impractical for my too-tall, too-thin frame. And the song appears on Knowles&#x2019;s recent album, &#x201C;A Seat at the Table,&#x201D; after an interlude featuring the rapper Master P., whose stories of success as an independent music artist and executive help provide thematic shape to the project. For many of us who were young and black, or young and black and Southern, like Knowles, during the years when he dominated the rap charts, his story has always been inspirational. &#x201C;If you don&#x2019;t understand my record, you don&#x2019;t understand me, so this is not for you,&#x201D; he says.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Songs that can be described as &#x201C;black-empowerment music&#x201D; make up a genre as diverse as that of black music itself. There are the time-tested tracks that focus on pride, like James Brown&#x2019;s &#x201C;Say It Loud &#x2014; I&#x2019;m Black and I&#x2019;m Proud,&#x201D; and Nina Simone&#x2019;s &#x201C;Young, Gifted and Black.&#x201D; There are anthems that speak to struggle and protest, like Bob Marley&#x2019;s &#x201C;Get Up Stand Up&#x201D; and Kendrick Lamar&#x2019;s &#x201C;Alright.&#x201D; And there are a plethora of doggedly sunny ditties like Nas&#x2019;s &#x201C;I Can&#x201D; and Jaheim&#x2019;s &#x201C;Fabulous.&#x201D; Knowles&#x2019;s &#x201C;F.U.B.U.&#x201D; hews closer to the first two categories, and a majority of its messages of pride and protest are nuanced, centering the individual over the collective. She allows that not every black person will relate to the experiences she recounts: &#x201C;When you feeling all alone/And you can&#x2019;t even be you up in your home/When you even feeling it from your own.&#x201D; The song pushes past one litmus test for the black-empowerment anthem &#x2014; that it present itself as made for black listeners, broadly &#x2014; and tries to reach an even more elusive audience: the black listener who maybe hasn&#x2019;t felt included in such messages before.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Art finds who it finds, and the white gaze lands where it lands. The more you try to ignore it, the more it seems keen on dissecting you. Knowles is aware of this. The deliberate rejection of white scrutiny is part of a long tradition of black art-making. In his famous 1926 essay, &#x201C;The Negro Artist and the Racial Mountain,&#x201D; which would serve as the young poet&#x2019;s artistic manifesto, Langston Hughes declared that younger black artists should create &#x201C;without fear or shame. If white people are pleased, we are glad. If they are not, it doesn&#x2019;t matter.&#x201D; It&#x2019;s the sort of thing emerging black writers &#x2014; me included &#x2014; have to remind themselves when putting pen to paper, lest we succumb to pandering for white attention.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">In &#x201C;F.U.B.U.&#x201D; Knowles doesn&#x2019;t just eschew the white gaze for the sake of creating her art; she performs that rejection in song, to pull her black listeners close. It&#x2019;s a rhetorical invitation for black folks to grab a chair and settle in for real talk about lives like theirs. To her white listeners (who are listening, after all), she offers a tongue-in-cheek indictment as consolation: &#x201C;Don&#x2019;t feel bad if you can&#x2019;t sing along/Just be glad you got the whole wide world.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">The verses in &#x201C;F.U.B.U.&#x201D; outline microaggressions, but the song isn&#x2019;t &#x201C;about&#x201D; microaggression; it&#x2019;s about recognition. &#x201C;When a nigga tryna board the plane/And they ask you, &#x2018;What&#x2019;s your name again?&#x2019;/&#x2019;Cause they thinking, Yeah, you&#x2019;re all the same,&#x201D; Knowles sings. This is the sonic equivalent of shaking out your hair after long hours of wearing it pulled back and tied down for work, putting on your sweatpants and calling your girlfriend to tell her about the day you had. Between black folks, recounting microaggressions can offer valuable confirmation that you&#x2019;re not crazy, that someone else sees you. It&#x2019;s commiseration, but without the misery, because the slights are both hurtful and numbingly commonplace. Knowles isn&#x2019;t listing racial slights on &#x201C;F.U.B.U.&#x201D; to educate those who perpetuate them; she does it to foster intimacy with her listeners.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">I first heard the song at the gym, on a cheap pair of headphones, so the bass on the record wasn&#x2019;t at the forefront as it should have been. The warbled notes of the piano and organ sounded muffled, as if underwater. On even very good headphones, the song&#x2019;s dragged-down pace is reminiscent of screw music &#x2014; the slow-motion, purposefully warped aesthetic born in Knowles&#x2019;s native Houston. The featured artists are a nod to R.&amp;B. over the past two decades. The-Dream sings in an airy falsetto, making the line &#x201C;I didn&#x2019;t come back down to earth to die&#x201D; sound resolute instead of bitter. The relative newcomer BJ the Chicago Kid&#x2019;s delivery &#x2014; by turns exuberant and plaintive &#x2014; is like that of a soloist during Sunday praise and worship. The backing vocals by Tweet are a special treat for those of us who sometimes shake our fists at the sky, wondering what ever happened to that singer and her hypnotizing voice.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">For all its angst and exasperation, &#x201C;F.U.B.U.&#x201D; is a party song, just not one for the nightclub. Its measured cadence and dragging bass are perfect for a spontaneous, low-key house party. You might be able to pull off a drawn-out, stylized two-step to &#x201C;F.U.B.U.,&#x201D; but really it&#x2019;s a rhythm tailored to the simple head-nod &#x2014; an appropriate move for a song about recognition. Its boisterous horns call to mind the New Orleans second line, those musical parades marched both for celebration and for mourning.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Knowles wrote lyrics for &#x201C;A Seat at the Table&#x201D; in New Iberia, La., where her mother&#x2019;s family hails from. Her mother, Tina Lawson, has said that her family was essentially run out of town following a salt-mine collapse involving her father. In the contentious aftermath, a Molotov cocktail was thrown through the window of their home. They didn&#x2019;t go far &#x2014; less than 300 miles west to Galveston, Tex. Hasty separation leaves a generational longing all the same. It follows that an album created in a town once fled for fear of white violence should include &#x201C;F.U.B.U.,&#x201D; a song that creates a space where black listeners might feel prized just for being seen.&#x2666;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><em>Angela Flournoy is the author of &#x201C;The Turner House&#x201D; and a fellow at the New York Public Library&#x2019;s Cullman Center.</em></p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n          <div class=\"read-more\">Read more</div>\n        </div>\n      </section>\n      \n      <div class=\"m-ad-container\"></div>\n      \n    \n      <section id=\"/ariana-grande-side-to-side\" data-id=\"ariana-grande-side-to-side\"\n        class=\"m-section  ariana-grande\">\n        \n        <header class=\"m-header\">\n          <div class=\"m-header-meta\">\n            <h2 class=\"m-number\">17<span class=\"dot\">.<span></h2>\n            \n            <h2 class=\"m-headline\">\n              <span class=\"m-player\" data-slug=\"ariana-grande-side-to-side\">\n                <audio class=\"m-audio\" src=\"https://p.scdn.co/mp3-preview/6d750113190016f8eaf858e150fc2d9135c4c4fa?cid=null\"></audio>\n              </span>\n              <span class=\"m-headline-text\">Side to Side</span>\n            </h2>\n            <h4 class=\"m-artist\">Ariana Grande</h4>\n            \n            <div class=\"m-header-footer\">\n              <p class=\"m-leadin\">Bubble-gum-pop queen turns tongue-in-cheek sex bomb.</p>\n              <p class=\"m-byline\">By HAZEL CILLS</p>\n            </div>\n            <div class=\"tools-wrapper\">\n              <div class=\"m-sharetools\" data-shares=\"facebook,twitter\"\n                data-url=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2017/03/09/magazine/25-songs-that-tell-us-where-music-is-going.html#/ariana-grande-side-to-side\"\n                data-title=\"What Ariana Grande's work can tell us about where music is headed.\"\n                data-description=\"\"></div>\n              \n              <a class=\"m-full-track-link\" href=\"https://open.spotify.com/track/1pKeFVVUOPjFsOABub0OaV\" target=\"_blank\">\n                <img class=\"spotify-icon\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2017/03/03/music2017/assets/icons/spotify.svg\" />Full Track\n              </a>\n              \n            </div>\n            \n          </div>\n        </header>\n        \n        <div class=\"content \">\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">When a young pop star outgrows the wholesome, teeny-bopper sound that made her career, we tend to watch the results while peeking through our fingers, terrified of what we&#x2019;ll see. This is how we watched as Christina Aguilera put on her &#x201C;Dirrty&#x201D; leather chaps, as Britney Spears stepped out of her &#x201C;little girl&#x201D; world on &#x201C;I&#x2019;m a Slave 4 U,&#x201D; as Miley Cyrus awkwardly fraternized with a stripper pole at the Teen Choice Awards. What makes these moments so cringeworthy is their self-seriousness, their declaration that to be adult is to be sexy &#x2014; and to be sexy is to be straight-faced, preferably with a well-oiled body, writhing and pining for male approval.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Ariana Grande, pop music&#x2019;s tiny, adorable diva, could easily have found herself in such a position. A former Broadway actress and the former star of the Nickelodeon show &#x201C;Sam &amp; Cat,&#x201D; she has an unabashed theater-kid spirit and a flair for costume &#x2014; you often find her wearing cat ears and thigh-high vinyl boots. Her songs are brassy, retro numbers that deal with gushy PG-13 love, and she has a tendency to oversell them with the zeal of a collegiate a cappella singer, her impressive vocal range pushing against the edges of her bubble-gum hits as if trying to pop them entirely. Grande, still straddling the line between child star and adult hitmaker, is in the prime risk group for hypersexed transition songs.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            \n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  <figure\n  id=\"media-100000004969422\"\n  class=\"media photo horizontal jumbo\">\n  <div class=\"image\">\n    <img src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music17-grande/12music16-grande-master180.jpg\"\n    data-ratio=\"0.6666666666666666\"\n    data-pattern=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music17-grande/12music16-grande-{{size}}.jpg\"\n    data-widths='[{\"slug\":\"blogSmallInline\",\"size\":151},{\"slug\":\"master180\",\"size\":180},{\"slug\":\"blog225\",\"size\":225},{\"slug\":\"tmagSF\",\"size\":362},{\"slug\":\"blog427\",\"size\":427},{\"slug\":\"blog480\",\"size\":480},{\"slug\":\"master495\",\"size\":495},{\"slug\":\"blog533\",\"size\":533},{\"slug\":\"tmagArticle\",\"size\":592},{\"slug\":\"articleLarge\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"slide\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"popup\",\"size\":650},{\"slug\":\"master675\",\"size\":675},{\"slug\":\"jumbo\",\"size\":1024},{\"slug\":\"master1050\",\"size\":1050},{\"slug\":\"superJumbo\",\"size\":2048}]'\n    itemprop=\"url\"/>\n  </div>\n  <figcaption class=\"caption\">\n    <span class=\"caption-text\"></span>\n    <span class=\"credit\">Illustration by Delcan & Company</span>\n  </figcaption>\n</figure>\n\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">And on &#x201C;Side to Side,&#x201D; the biggest single from her excellent third album, &#x201C;Dangerous Woman,&#x201D; she&#x2019;s aiming for something grown-and-sexy. &#x201C;Side to Side&#x201D; has her tapping the same watered-down dancehall trend that fueled songs like Justin Bieber&#x2019;s &#x201C;Sorry&#x201D; and Drake&#x2019;s &#x201C;Controlla,&#x201D; with lyrics about sleeping with a bad secret boyfriend she keeps hidden from her friends. &#x201C;I&#x2019;ve been here all night/I&#x2019;ve been here all day/And boy got me walking side to side,&#x201D; she sings. Walking that way, Grande hinted in an interview last year with MTV &#x2014; just in case anyone didn&#x2019;t get it &#x2014; is a result of having too much sex, too vigorously.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">It&#x2019;s a huge hit. The song, in which Nicki Minaj coolly raps about riding a bicycle as if it were a male member (or vice versa), as if she were starring in some psychosexual Cronenberg horror film, has more than 420 million plays on Spotify. But &#x201C;Side to Side&#x201D; isn&#x2019;t exactly sexy. With its coy innuendo, as well as a video that uses SoulCycle-esque bikes as a metaphor for Grande&#x2019;s sexual workouts, &#x201C;Side to Side&#x201D; plays more like a giggly inside joke.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">We&#x2019;re in a golden age for things like this: media made by women, aimed at women, in which sex is explicit, messy and, most important, funny. Listening to &#x201C;Side to Side,&#x201D; I&#x2019;m reminded of Issa Rae&#x2019;s HBO series &#x201C;Insecure,&#x201D; in which Rae&#x2019;s eyes continually wander to an ex named Daniel, whom her best friend describes as Rae&#x2019;s Achilles&#x2019; heel &#x2014; except instead of &#x201C;heel,&#x201D; she uses a certain nickname for a phallus. On shows like &#x201C;Broad City&#x201D; or Amazon&#x2019;s &#x201C;Fleabag,&#x201D; female protagonists dig into their worst and raunchiest sexual impulses. Last year, the Swedish pop star Tove Lo named her record &#x201C;Lady Wood,&#x201D; a goofy, equal-opportunity reinvention of stereotypical male arousal.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">With its brazenly over-the-top premise, &#x201C;Side to Side&#x201D; joins them, beating you to the punch line about Grande&#x2019;s revamp as Sexy Adult. It doesn&#x2019;t feel as if it&#x2019;s designed to stir anyone&#x2019;s arousal &#x2014; only their laughter. Does this song sound ridiculous? Well, that&#x2019;s the point. In a genre that fetishizes sexual passion that lasts for eternity, Grande&#x2019;s song happily delivers comic realism, with a surprisingly smart wink.&#x2666;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><em>Hazel Cills is a staff writer for MTV News.</em></p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n          <div class=\"read-more\">Read more</div>\n        </div>\n      </section>\n      \n    \n      <section id=\"/kanye-west-fade\" data-id=\"kanye-west-fade\"\n        class=\"m-section  kanye-west\">\n        \n        <header class=\"m-header\">\n          <div class=\"m-header-meta\">\n            <h2 class=\"m-number\">18<span class=\"dot\">.<span></h2>\n            \n            <h2 class=\"m-headline\">\n              <span class=\"m-player\" data-slug=\"kanye-west-fade\">\n                <audio class=\"m-audio\" src=\"https://p.scdn.co/mp3-preview/906355de278856808a262d50a5a6673b56810c04?cid=null\"></audio>\n              </span>\n              <span class=\"m-headline-text\">Fade</span>\n            </h2>\n            <h4 class=\"m-artist\">Kanye West</h4>\n            \n            <div class=\"m-header-footer\">\n              <p class=\"m-leadin\">What would black music sound like in an alternate universe?</p>\n              <p class=\"m-byline\">By THOMAS CHATTERTON WILLIAMS</p>\n            </div>\n            <div class=\"tools-wrapper\">\n              <div class=\"m-sharetools\" data-shares=\"facebook,twitter\"\n                data-url=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2017/03/09/magazine/25-songs-that-tell-us-where-music-is-going.html#/kanye-west-fade\"\n                data-title=\"What Kanye West's work can tell us about where music is headed.\"\n                data-description=\"\"></div>\n              \n              <a class=\"m-full-track-link\" href=\"https://open.spotify.com/track/3cCxoOgfi6hgt8MNteuiiD\" target=\"_blank\">\n                <img class=\"spotify-icon\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2017/03/03/music2017/assets/icons/spotify.svg\" />Full Track\n              </a>\n              \n            </div>\n            \n          </div>\n        </header>\n        \n        <div class=\"content \">\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">The genius of &#x201C;Fade,&#x201D; the penultimate track on Kanye West&#x2019;s living work of art, &#x201C;The Life of Pablo,&#x201D; is evident from the opening lines, a sample from the white Motown group Rare Earth. But it was a half-minute in, at that first unmistakable rip of bass, that I lost my mind. Like many of West&#x2019;s songs, &#x201C;Fade&#x201D; is built around several commingling samples. Its rhythmic backbone is the deceptively simple arrangement from the 1985 classic &#x201C;Mystery of Love,&#x201D; by Larry Heard, better known as Mr. Fingers. That track, along with a handful of others, marks a seminal moment in the history of deep house &#x2014; a rich and criminally neglected chapter in the book of black music.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Today it&#x2019;s easy to forget that in the early and mid-&#x2019;80s there existed a window when New York rap, Chicago house and Detroit techno &#x2014; as well as a slew of other fledgling genres and subcultures &#x2014; functioned more or less as equals, each as likely as the next to flounder or thrive. New York won the contest handily, and now hip-hop has so thoroughly subsumed mainstream black culture that it often feels as if earlier artistic forms have either been eradicated or retrofitted to its preferences (see: funk, R.&amp;B. and jazz). House music &#x2014; much like West himself &#x2014; is unabashedly black and Chicago-bred, but somewhere along the line, it grew cozy in Europe and came to be seen as catering to white people. And though it has only ever managed to find significant audiences overseas, this transfixing style of minimal electronic dance music was pioneered by Midwestern D.J.s spinning mainly for black and gay audiences looking to &#x201C;jack&#x201D; their bodies at Windy City nightclubs like the Muzic Box and the Warehouse (where, under the stewardship of Frankie Knuckles, the style was birthed and named). While trailblazers like Mr. Fingers &#x2014; a virtuosic multi-instrumentalist &#x2014; are worshiped in London, Paris and Berlin, they are barely remembered back at home.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">&#x201C;Fade&#x201D; sets out to correct this. Onto the wide-open surface &#x201C;Mystery of Love&#x201D; provides, West spreads out his own sparse raps alongside what grows into an aural smorgasbord of samples, allusions and guest appearances spanning eras and ethnicities &#x2014; &#x2019;90s Nuyorican house, the white rapper/singer Post Malone &#x2014; a subtle reminder of the outsize influence of black aesthetics on all manner of American and global culture.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Which is why, as a radio-friendly hit (with an awe-inspiring video to boot), &#x201C;Fade&#x201D; feels not only generous but subversive: In the span of a little over three minutes, it gives the lie to simplistic conceptions of musical borders. West has always displayed a rare encyclopedic and intuitive grasp of both mainstream and regional black sounds, from traditional gospel and R.&amp;B. to college-inflected spoken word and even black Greek stepping, not to mention dance, reggae, trap and drill music. He knows that, glimpsed from the proper vantage, these are but facets of the same, constantly shifting whole. I don&#x2019;t think there is another pop star who could conceive of such a medley, let alone bring it to life in a way that coheres. Yet &#x201C;Fade&#x201D; doesn&#x2019;t just cohere; it functions as a sly and infectious meditation on the variety of formal possibilities of black sound &#x2014; as invented and interpreted by black people themselves as well as Latinos and white people. It also serves as a bittersweet thought experiment: Things could have been otherwise. Imagine, if you will, a world in which Mr. Fingers got his due.&#x2666;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><em>Thomas Chatterton Williams is a contributing writer for the magazine.</em></p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n          <div class=\"read-more\">Read more</div>\n        </div>\n      </section>\n      \n      <div class=\"m-ad-container\"></div>\n      \n    \n      <section id=\"/ccile-mclorin-salvant-trolley-song\" data-id=\"ccile-mclorin-salvant-trolley-song\"\n        class=\"m-section feature c-cile-mclorin-salvant\">\n        \n        <header class=\"m-header\">\n          <div class=\"m-header-meta\">\n            <h2 class=\"m-number\">19<span class=\"dot\">.<span></h2>\n            \n            <h2 class=\"m-headline\">\n              <span class=\"m-player\" data-slug=\"ccile-mclorin-salvant-trolley-song\">\n                <audio class=\"m-audio\" src=\"https://p.scdn.co/mp3-preview/40b64674be7c766dc0439e07458c6dd63da61b02?cid=null\"></audio>\n              </span>\n              <span class=\"m-headline-text\">Trolley Song</span>\n            </h2>\n            \n            <div class=\"m-header-footer\">\n              <p class=\"m-leadin\">The counter-diva who brings a comedian&#x2019;s timing and sensibility to jazz.</p>\n              <p class=\"m-byline\">By MARGO JEFFERSON</p>\n            </div>\n            <div class=\"tools-wrapper\">\n              <div class=\"m-sharetools\" data-shares=\"facebook,twitter\"\n                data-url=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2017/03/09/magazine/25-songs-that-tell-us-where-music-is-going.html#/ccile-mclorin-salvant-trolley-song\"\n                data-title=\"What C\u00e9cile McLorin Salvant's work can tell us about where music is headed.\"\n                data-description=\"\"></div>\n              \n              <a class=\"m-full-track-link\" href=\"https://open.spotify.com/track/5j78Obp9QYGopCUlsDd3nQ\" target=\"_blank\">\n                <img class=\"spotify-icon\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2017/03/03/music2017/assets/icons/spotify.svg\" />Full Track\n              </a>\n              \n            </div>\n            \n              <h4 class=\"m-artist\">C\u00e9cile McLorin Salvant</h4>\n            \n          </div>\n        </header>\n        \n        <div class=\"content \">\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><span class=\"dropcap\">H</span>ow can you tell the singer from the song? On a good day, you can&#x2019;t &#x2014; so let&#x2019;s start with the singer.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">She&#x2019;s C&#xE9;cile McLorin Salvant, a 27-year-old with an exhilarating command of the jazz vocal tradition, which has long been dominated by women, many of them black. Salvant&#x2019;s parents are French-Guadeloupean and Haitian; she grew up in Miami studying classical music, then moved to Paris and added jazz to her studies. In 2010, she won the Thelonious Monk Institute International Jazz Vocals Competition; three albums later, &#x201C;For One to Love&#x201D; won the jazz vocal album of the year at the 2016 Grammy Awards.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">I said she&#x2019;s in &#x201C;command of&#x201D; the jazz tradition. Better to say she&#x2019;s in communion with it. I like how she listens. I like how she tests herself and learns as she performs. Salvant has a supple, well-trained voice with spot-on pitch. (No vibrato-teases; no meandering warbles passing as melisma.) Her low notes go from husky to full-bodied; her high notes float purely and cleanly. When she scats, it&#x2019;s not an ego trip but a musical game, where notes and syllables get to shape-shift.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            \n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  <figure\n  id=\"media-100000004967594\"\n  class=\"media photo vertical jumbo\">\n  <div class=\"image\">\n    <img src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music6-salvant1/12music6-salvant1-master180-v3.jpg\"\n    data-ratio=\"1.2722222222222221\"\n    data-pattern=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music6-salvant1/12music6-salvant1-{{size}}-v3.jpg\"\n    data-widths='[{\"slug\":\"blogSmallInline\",\"size\":151},{\"slug\":\"master180\",\"size\":180},{\"slug\":\"blog225\",\"size\":225},{\"slug\":\"tmagSF\",\"size\":362},{\"slug\":\"slide\",\"size\":394},{\"slug\":\"popup\",\"size\":394},{\"slug\":\"blog427\",\"size\":427},{\"slug\":\"blog480\",\"size\":480},{\"slug\":\"master495\",\"size\":495},{\"slug\":\"blog533\",\"size\":533},{\"slug\":\"tmagArticle\",\"size\":592},{\"slug\":\"articleLarge\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"master675\",\"size\":675},{\"slug\":\"jumbo\",\"size\":807},{\"slug\":\"master1050\",\"size\":1050},{\"slug\":\"superJumbo\",\"size\":1613}]'\n    itemprop=\"url\"/>\n  </div>\n  <figcaption class=\"caption\">\n    <span class=\"caption-text\"></span>\n    <span class=\"credit\">Erik Madigan Heck for The New York Times</span>\n  </figcaption>\n</figure>\n\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Like many young jazz singers, she does the Great American Songbook &#x2014; the Gershwins, Rodgers and Hart or Hammerstein, Sondheim, Ellington. The risk? Sounding decorous and derivative. Like some other young jazz singers, she does the black vaudeville hits of Bert Williams and Bessie Smith, even some of the exotica that female musicians once tossed out to keep their fans tantalized. Here the risk is archness: the knowing postmodern wink.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">I wouldn&#x2019;t be writing about Salvant if she had fallen into either trap. But mainstream success has other traps. And while it has been a long time since jazz was at the center of pop commerce, the star- (or cult-) making machinery still labors to produce familiar types. Especially for women, and even more especially for black women.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">For black women in pop music, the dominant and preferred model remains the Diva. Here&#x2019;s my personal theory. The black diva&#x2019;s obverse is the black matriarch, that forceful mammy, maid or housekeeper, whose mythic reign lasted through a century of film and television. And yes, it&#x2019;s a long way from mammy to diva, but there&#x2019;s one constant. Racially and socially, this figure is considered lower and lesser. Theatrically, then &#x2014; it&#x2019;s the law of fantasy compensation &#x2014; she must appear greater. In the old days, that meant she was literally bigger, louder, bossier. Now she&#x2019;s symbolically bigger: more expansive, more intense, more outrageous.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Call the roll of the last half-century, from Patti LaBelle to Fantasia, Jennifer Holliday to Jennifer Hudson. My favorites are Aretha Franklin and Tina Turner because of their contradictions. There&#x2019;s a core reserve, even sadness in Franklin, while Turner channeled her lust-goddess intensity through the dance moves of a gritty pugilist. Beyonc&#xE9; and Rihanna are the grandest of contemporary divas. Their social, financial and cultural riches are vast; they command global kingdoms. They aren&#x2019;t prey to traditional diva narratives of abuse or self-destruction; they turn all vulnerabilities into victories. Beyonc&#xE9; is the gracious sovereign, Rihanna the cocky bad girl turned It Girl. But they share a mandate: The Black Diva must flaunt, court and rule.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Jazz divas have tended to have alter egos. Billie Holiday was a laconic wit before she was the Lady with the Gardenia; Sarah Vaughan could counter her Divine One with up-tempo Sassy. Dinah (The Queen) Washington was also a salty good-time gal. I hear their traces in Salvant&#x2019;s singing, and it gives me great pleasure to watch her revise the tropes of black divadom. Don&#x2019;t misunderstand &#x2014; she&#x2019;s not an anti-diva. I&#x2019;d call her a counter-diva: the heroine as ebullient comedian.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            \n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  <figure\n  id=\"media-100000004967845\"\n  class=\"media photo horizontal jumbo\">\n  <div class=\"image\">\n    <img src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music6-salvant2/12music-salvant2-master180.jpg\"\n    data-ratio=\"0.6666666666666666\"\n    data-pattern=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music6-salvant2/12music-salvant2-{{size}}.jpg\"\n    data-widths='[{\"slug\":\"blogSmallInline\",\"size\":151},{\"slug\":\"master180\",\"size\":180},{\"slug\":\"blog225\",\"size\":225},{\"slug\":\"tmagSF\",\"size\":362},{\"slug\":\"blog427\",\"size\":427},{\"slug\":\"blog480\",\"size\":480},{\"slug\":\"master495\",\"size\":495},{\"slug\":\"blog533\",\"size\":533},{\"slug\":\"tmagArticle\",\"size\":592},{\"slug\":\"articleLarge\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"slide\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"popup\",\"size\":650},{\"slug\":\"master675\",\"size\":675},{\"slug\":\"jumbo\",\"size\":1024},{\"slug\":\"master1050\",\"size\":1050},{\"slug\":\"superJumbo\",\"size\":2048}]'\n    itemprop=\"url\"/>\n  </div>\n  <figcaption class=\"caption\">\n    <span class=\"caption-text\">C\u00e9cile McLorin Salvant at the Chicago Jazz Festival in 2014.</span>\n    <span class=\"credit\">Paul Natkin/Getty Images</span>\n  </figcaption>\n</figure>\n\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><strong>Listen to her </strong>version of &#x201C;The Trolley Song&#x201D; from the 1944 hit film &#x201C;Meet Me In St. Louis.&#x201D; This is a song that could give any singer acute Anxiety of Influence symptoms. It began as Judy Garland&#x2019;s clarion love call, then entered the jazz repertory to be taken up by Sarah Vaughan and Betty Carter. Vaughan&#x2019;s version is speedy and pristinely seductive; Carter&#x2019;s is a daredevil blend of swing and abstract expressionism. Salvant pays homage to all three with touches of loving parody &#x2014; Garland&#x2019;s girlishness, Vaughan&#x2019;s love for her own silky vibrato, Carter&#x2019;s near-manic tempo changes &#x2014; finding her own interpretation. The train rhythms stop, start and stutter; so does Salvant&#x2019;s voice. Our heroine is thinking and feeling her way, note by note, word by word, into exuberant infatuation, fashioning a romantic-comedy monologue in which the woman surprises herself with each turn of phrase and tempo.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Salvant, like all counter-divas, constructs her look with care. Hers is gamine glam: Her face is round, her hair close-cropped. She wears big, blocky white glasses &#x2014; a droll trademark, like Fats Waller&#x2019;s derby; a red fascinator with feathers that look like insect feelers.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">The black counter-diva is now making her way into the culture at large. I hear her when the mezzo-soprano Alicia Hall Moran builds a song around nothing but a repeated &#x201C;Shhhh&#x201D; or arranges to fuse John Dowland&#x2019;s &#x201C;Flow My Tears&#x201D; and Smokey Robinson&#x2019;s &#x201C;Cruisin&#x2019;.&#x201D; She&#x2019;s in the mischief that snakes through Corinne Bailey Rae&#x2019;s rueful feathery songs too.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">I see her when Tracee Ellis Ross goes goofy on &#x201C;black-ish.&#x201D; (It&#x2019;s as if she has reclaimed the soubrette wit her mother renounced for lofty grandeur.) I like how Rashida Jones uses cheerful slapstick to counter her sweet-girl prettiness in &#x201C;Angie Tribeca.&#x201D; As Lucca Quinn, the clever lawyer on &#x201C;The Good Wife&#x201D; and &#x201C;The Good Fight,&#x201D; Cush Jumbo seems to be letting us in on a private joke; a tiny pause, the hint of a double-take, suggests a quizzical &#x201C;Can I get away with this?&#x201D; and a gleeful &#x201C;OMG, I just did.&#x201D; And won&#x2019;t it be fun to watch Lupita Nyong&#x2019;o in the film version of Chimamanda Adiche&#x2019;s &#x201C;Americanah&#x201D;? Freed from period dramas that stretch from slavery to the Empire galaxy, she will bring her talents to a here-and-now comedy of morals and manners.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">John Gielgud once said that style is knowing what play you&#x2019;re in. It&#x2019;s true for all performers who work with varied traditions and repertories. And it means that the performer must know what age she is performing in, and why. Along with a merry band of counter-divas, Salvant is thinking hard about what conventions, habits and desires need revising &#x2014; in her art, and in her audience.&#x2666;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><em>Margo Jefferson is the author of &#x201C;Negroland: A Memoir.&#x201D;</em></p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n          <div class=\"read-more\">Read more</div>\n        </div>\n      </section>\n      \n    \n      <section id=\"/lady-gaga-grigio-girls\" data-id=\"lady-gaga-grigio-girls\"\n        class=\"m-section  lady-gaga\">\n        \n        <header class=\"m-header\">\n          <div class=\"m-header-meta\">\n            <h2 class=\"m-number\">20<span class=\"dot\">.<span></h2>\n            \n            <h2 class=\"m-headline\">\n              <span class=\"m-player\" data-slug=\"lady-gaga-grigio-girls\">\n                <audio class=\"m-audio\" src=\"https://p.scdn.co/mp3-preview/54e6c3a689d173e2589299776962c874b5fd0de5?cid=null\"></audio>\n              </span>\n              <span class=\"m-headline-text\">Grigio Girls</span>\n            </h2>\n            <h4 class=\"m-artist\">Lady Gaga</h4>\n            \n            <div class=\"m-header-footer\">\n              <p class=\"m-leadin\">A boozy toast to friendship.</p>\n              <p class=\"m-byline\">By BOBBY FINGER</p>\n            </div>\n            <div class=\"tools-wrapper\">\n              <div class=\"m-sharetools\" data-shares=\"facebook,twitter\"\n                data-url=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2017/03/09/magazine/25-songs-that-tell-us-where-music-is-going.html#/lady-gaga-grigio-girls\"\n                data-title=\"What Lady Gaga's work can tell us about where music is headed.\"\n                data-description=\"\"></div>\n              \n              <a class=\"m-full-track-link\" href=\"https://open.spotify.com/track/6Xxvdy1m9TX9HTxOzQcfuV\" target=\"_blank\">\n                <img class=\"spotify-icon\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2017/03/03/music2017/assets/icons/spotify.svg\" />Full Track\n              </a>\n              \n            </div>\n            \n          </div>\n        </header>\n        \n        <div class=\"content \">\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">When Lady Gaga, draped in what looked like the silver uniform for a sport that hadn&#x2019;t yet been invented, <a href=\"https://youtu.be/txXwg712zw4\">jumped off the roof</a> of NRG Stadium at the Super Bowl this year, she reaped the benefits of a safe-but-exciting performance almost immediately. Fireworks shows and rip-roaring acrobatics and dancing in front of a group of second-rate backup dancers in capes are the kind of broadly appealing theatrics that generate awe without shock, intrigue without the threat of controversy. Her albums shot to the top of the music charts, her performance was roundly praised by fans and critics and more than 111 million viewers were reminded that her 2009 single &#x201C;Bad Romance&#x201D; still bangs.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">This wasn&#x2019;t Lady Gaga&#x2019;s comeback, as many claimed: She never really left us, nor had she abandoned the music that made her famous.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Two of Gaga&#x2019;s early albums, &#x201C;The Fame&#x201D; (2008) and &#x201C;Born This Way&#x201D; (2011), were bursting at the seams with instantly memorable dance-pop hits. But after her fourth solo album, the both fairly and unfairly maligned &#x201C;Artpop&#x201D; (2013), failed to deliver that kind of instant success, Gaga redirected her focus from an ostentatious existence &#x2014; she wore a dress of raw beef to the 2010 MTV Video Music Awards, after all &#x2014; to less-bombastic acts of musical bravura that showcased her virtuosic skills as both a singer and a pianist. Last October, she released her much-anticipated fifth album, the deeply personal and country-tinged &#x201C;Joanne.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">So when she fell to Earth during last month&#x2019;s Super Bowl and performed the hits that made her famous, fans who thought &#x201C;Joanne,&#x201D; with its stripped-down production and abundance of twang, was yet another sign that she had abandoned her old ways were able to breathe a sigh of relief: Gaga&#x2019;s verve hadn&#x2019;t gone anywhere. It&#x2019;s a message she made clear in &#x201C;Grigio Girls,&#x201D; a midtempo acoustic bonus track and the album&#x2019;s best song.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">In &#x201C;Grigio Girls,&#x201D; Gaga recalls a moment in her early 20s when she was &#x201C;spiraling out,&#x201D; only to be saved by the contagious spirit of her 30-something friend Sonja Durham, who was battling breast cancer at the time. &#x201C;Grigio Girls&#x201D; is an ode to friendship and singing along to your favorite songs off-key. But through some strange alchemy involving pop-culture references and cheap wine, it manages to do more than just celebrate sisterhood: It&#x2019;s proof that Gaga is still fundamentally Gaga, whether she&#x2019;s wrapped in meat or the arm of her recent collaborator, Tony Bennett. Though explicitly about Dunham and the inspirational &#x201C;tough girl&#x201D; persona she shared with Joanne Germanotta (Gaga&#x2019;s aunt, who died in 1974 and for whom the album is named), &#x201C;Grigio Girls&#x201D; becomes something more universal once Gaga&#x2019;s own voice is joined by the backing harmony. &#x201C;Sisters never pack up,&#x201D; the singers reassure us. &#x201C;We always run back.&#x201D;&#x2666;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><em>Bobby Finger is a staff writer for Jezebel and co-host of &#x201C;Who? Weekly.&#x201D;</em></p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n          <div class=\"read-more\">Read more</div>\n        </div>\n      </section>\n      \n      <div class=\"m-ad-container\"></div>\n      \n    \n      <section id=\"/young-ma-ooouuu\" data-id=\"young-ma-ooouuu\"\n        class=\"m-section feature young-m-a\">\n        \n        <header class=\"m-header\">\n          <div class=\"m-header-meta\">\n            <h2 class=\"m-number\">21<span class=\"dot\">.<span></h2>\n            \n            <h2 class=\"m-headline\">\n              <span class=\"m-player\" data-slug=\"young-ma-ooouuu\">\n                <audio class=\"m-audio\" src=\"https://p.scdn.co/mp3-preview/0852b011bb11c251d0e9f90c6a010997013e0f09?cid=null\"></audio>\n              </span>\n              <span class=\"m-headline-text\">Ooouuu</span>\n            </h2>\n            \n            <div class=\"m-header-footer\">\n              <p class=\"m-leadin\">A woman&#x2019;s unabashed appreciation of women.</p>\n              <p class=\"m-byline\">By HANNAH GIORGIS</p>\n            </div>\n            <div class=\"tools-wrapper\">\n              <div class=\"m-sharetools\" data-shares=\"facebook,twitter\"\n                data-url=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2017/03/09/magazine/25-songs-that-tell-us-where-music-is-going.html#/young-ma-ooouuu\"\n                data-title=\"What Young M.A's work can tell us about where music is headed.\"\n                data-description=\"\"></div>\n              \n              <a class=\"m-full-track-link\" href=\"https://open.spotify.com/track/0D21XvHcVsIvJM6FcGY2BT\" target=\"_blank\">\n                <img class=\"spotify-icon\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2017/03/03/music2017/assets/icons/spotify.svg\" />Full Track\n              </a>\n              \n            </div>\n            \n              <h4 class=\"m-artist\">Young M.A</h4>\n            \n          </div>\n        </header>\n        \n        <div class=\"content \">\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><span class=\"dropcap\">Y</span>oung M.A&#x2019;s music sounds like summer: It buzzes, bumps, burns. It&#x2019;s hot. &#x201C;OOOUUU,&#x201D; the Brooklyn artist&#x2019;s single from last May, both soundtracked the sweltering season and cemented its place in party rotations well into the colder months, and well beyond New York. It finds the confident East New York-born newcomer relaxed, but not sedate. Her opening line is &#x201C;Yo, bro, I think I had too much Hennessy, man&#x201D; &#x2014; and M.A has said she was intentionally &#x201C;smizz and drizz&#x201D; (that is, drunk) while recording it, &#x201C;so it feels authentic.&#x201D; Hennessy is not known for encouraging rest, and M.A rides the beat with an energy that is at once frenetic and hazy. A slew of clever punch lines invite you to rap alongside her &#x2014; to feel yourself too. And with spacey, futuristic production from U-Dub of NY Bangers, &#x201C;OOOUUU&#x201D; insists you respect its groove by dancing with your whole body. When you hear it in the club, muscle memory takes over.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Like some of the best New York rap that preceded it, the track emboldens. M.A told the website Genius that the song wasn&#x2019;t modeled after fellow Brooklyn rapper Bobby Shmurda&#x2019;s &#x201C;Hot Nigga,&#x201D; but the two share similar beats &#x2014; and &#x201C;OOOUUU&#x201D; lends itself perfectly to the &#x201C;Shmoney Dance&#x201D; that took over Vine in 2014 and turned Shmurda into a viral star. The city&#x2019;s rappers took note: Less than a year since its release, the track has been remixed by local heavyweights like Nicki Minaj, French Montana, 50 Cent, Jadakiss and Remy Ma.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            \n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  <figure\n  id=\"media-100000004967596\"\n  class=\"media photo horizontal jumbo\">\n  <div class=\"image\">\n    <img src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music8-YOUNGMA/12music8-YOUNGMA-master180-v2.jpg\"\n    data-ratio=\"0.6666666666666666\"\n    data-pattern=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music8-YOUNGMA/12music8-YOUNGMA-{{size}}-v2.jpg\"\n    data-widths='[{\"slug\":\"blogSmallInline\",\"size\":151},{\"slug\":\"master180\",\"size\":180},{\"slug\":\"blog225\",\"size\":225},{\"slug\":\"tmagSF\",\"size\":362},{\"slug\":\"blog427\",\"size\":427},{\"slug\":\"blog480\",\"size\":480},{\"slug\":\"master495\",\"size\":495},{\"slug\":\"blog533\",\"size\":533},{\"slug\":\"tmagArticle\",\"size\":592},{\"slug\":\"articleLarge\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"slide\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"popup\",\"size\":650},{\"slug\":\"master675\",\"size\":675},{\"slug\":\"jumbo\",\"size\":1024},{\"slug\":\"master1050\",\"size\":1050},{\"slug\":\"superJumbo\",\"size\":1500}]'\n    itemprop=\"url\"/>\n  </div>\n  <figcaption class=\"caption\">\n    <span class=\"caption-text\"></span>\n    <span class=\"credit\">Erik Madigan Heck for The New York Times. Stylist: Jasmine Benjamin. Grooming: Red Phallon.</span>\n  </figcaption>\n</figure>\n\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">The original song is full of references to M.A&#x2019;s own life in Brooklyn, where she&#x2019;s &#x201C;in these streets more than Sesame.&#x201D; While the track&#x2019;s narrative might span only one day, it feels firmly grounded in the habitual: Young M.A raps what she knows. She&#x2019;s not only a woman rapping but also a masculine-presenting queer woman in an industry that is dominated by heterosexual men. Queer hip-hop artists are hardly new; rappers like Le1f, Zebra Katz, Mykki Blanco, Cakes Da Killa and many others have been at the forefront of shifting the genre&#x2019;s landscape in recent years, and rumors of sexual fluidity have always surrounded many of hip-hop&#x2019;s biggest stars, both despite and because of the industry&#x2019;s reputation for homophobia. (Queer women in particular must grapple with intersecting axes of misogyny and homophobia.) Still, M.A seems refreshing, and quintessentially New York, in her frankness. She is who she is.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">The &#x201C;OOOUUU&#x201D; punch lines that inspire the most raucous singalongs are those in which she speaks, openly and matter-of-factly, about sex with another woman. Rapping about women is not the entirety of her catalog, nor is it a gimmick; she simply traffics in the same kind of braggadocio the industry&#x2019;s men are often known for, which is to say she&#x2019;s your average Bed-Stuy stud. M.A is seductive &#x2014; even to women who don&#x2019;t identify as lesbian, queer or bisexual &#x2014; and she knows it. If M.A&#x2019;s contemporary Remy Ma is conceited, M.A is cocky: &#x201C;If that&#x2019;s your chick, then why she textin&#x2019; me?/Why she keep calling my phone speaking sexually?&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">The lines that follow are salacious, but they&#x2019;re not performative. M.A may be a commanding, bite-her-lip-when-she-looks-in-your-direction queer woman, but her music neither starts nor ends there. What&#x2019;s more significant than M.A&#x2019;s queerness is the way she deftly weaves references to sex with women into her music without overstating or explaining them. If you know, you know. And if you don&#x2019;t, you can still enjoy her music &#x2014; but M.A isn&#x2019;t going to waste her bars enlightening you.&#x2666;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><em>Hannah Giorgis is the special-projects editor for The Ringer.</em></p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n          <div class=\"read-more\">Read more</div>\n        </div>\n      </section>\n      \n    \n      <section id=\"/charles-bradley-changes\" data-id=\"charles-bradley-changes\"\n        class=\"m-section  charles-bradley\">\n        \n        <header class=\"m-header\">\n          <div class=\"m-header-meta\">\n            <h2 class=\"m-number\">22<span class=\"dot\">.<span></h2>\n            \n            <h2 class=\"m-headline\">\n              <span class=\"m-player\" data-slug=\"charles-bradley-changes\">\n                <audio class=\"m-audio\" src=\"https://p.scdn.co/mp3-preview/51da26c057f05cdef90da3865635e262699a2c9f?cid=null\"></audio>\n              </span>\n              <span class=\"m-headline-text\">Changes</span>\n            </h2>\n            <h4 class=\"m-artist\">Charles Bradley</h4>\n            \n            <div class=\"m-header-footer\">\n              <p class=\"m-leadin\">A &#x2019;70s rock star&#x2019;s troubles, and a 68-year-old soul singer&#x2019;s.</p>\n              <p class=\"m-byline\">By CHARLES AARON</p>\n            </div>\n            <div class=\"tools-wrapper\">\n              <div class=\"m-sharetools\" data-shares=\"facebook,twitter\"\n                data-url=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2017/03/09/magazine/25-songs-that-tell-us-where-music-is-going.html#/charles-bradley-changes\"\n                data-title=\"What Charles Bradley's work can tell us about where music is headed.\"\n                data-description=\"\"></div>\n              \n              <a class=\"m-full-track-link\" href=\"https://open.spotify.com/track/3i85UIF4nR7qZXTnEwqdPr\" target=\"_blank\">\n                <img class=\"spotify-icon\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2017/03/03/music2017/assets/icons/spotify.svg\" />Full Track\n              </a>\n              \n            </div>\n            \n          </div>\n        </header>\n        \n        <div class=\"content \">\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">A chord from a Hammond B3 organ quavers, and Charles Bradley&#x2019;s head snaps up, his eyes locking with the camera. When the woozy accompaniment hits, his deep-creased face wrenches; he shakes his head, places his palms together and gazes Godward. So begins the remarkable video for Bradley&#x2019;s soul rendition of Black Sabbath&#x2019;s 1972 gloom-folk lament &#x201C;Changes&#x201D; &#x2014; no lip-syncing, just the 68-year-old singer in a black turtleneck against a blank backdrop. For six minutes, as his sculpted rasp wails, Bradley uses his face and hands to silently act out a vast grief, one at which the plain-spoken lyrics, originally written about the Sabbath drummer Bill Ward&#x2019;s breakup with his first wife, only hint. Bradley conceived his version while comforting and reconciling with his dying mother, but even if you didn&#x2019;t know that, his pantomime would still rip you up.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">&#x201C;Changes&#x201D; was originally the product of rock&#x2019;s early-&#x2019;70s commercial largess. After three albums spent forging heavy metal in the unglamorous English Midlands, Sabbath was afforded a Bel Air mansion to record its fourth. The house was filled with equipment and enough drugs to get &#x201C;coked out of our brains every day,&#x201D; as the bassist Geezer Butler recalled in &#x201C;Black Sabbath: Doom Let Loose,&#x201D; Martin Popoff&#x2019;s illustrated history of the band. One debauched night, the guitarist Tony Iommi wandered over to a grand piano in the manse&#x2019;s ballroom and tapped out the melody of &#x201C;Changes.&#x201D; Butler wrote the lyrics, and Ozzy Osbourne, the singer, found some gentle nuance in them, despite being in such a drug-freaked state that he was &#x201C;dreaming there was, like, a tape machine coming into my room and eating me.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">When Bradley&#x2019;s guitarist, Tom Brenneck, raised the idea of covering &#x201C;Changes,&#x201D; he had never heard of Black Sabbath. &#x201C;But as I was learning the song, my mom was very ill, and she was talking to me a lot, and what she was saying fit the lyrics,&#x201D; he told me. &#x201C;Before that song, I was just trying to forget about her dying. But how can you forget the deepness of your mom finally telling you about her personal life and what she&#x2019;d gone through?&#x201D; Osbourne was so impressed by Bradley&#x2019;s version that he sent an appreciative email.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Bradley&#x2019;s relationship with his mother was deeply fraught. She left her 8-month-old son to move from Gainesville, Fla., to Brooklyn, then sent for him when he was 8. He left at 14, saying she was abusive. Decades later, she begged him to come home to Brooklyn and take care of her. He agreed, reluctantly, but time didn&#x2019;t soften his resentment. It was only when she finally shared the terror she experienced in the Jim Crow South of the 1930s and &#x2019;40s &#x2014; and her struggles as a single mother of three in Bed-Stuy &#x2014; that he was able to see his mother clearly.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">By 2011, when Bradley released the first of three top-shelf albums for the Brooklyn label Daptone, he was 62 and badly scarred by his own misfortunes. Even his ensuing, 11th-hour success has come at a cost: He has been pressured to support family and friends, and because his artistic method demands a deep emotional connection with his material, he has found himself revisiting buried memories of homelessness and violence. Late last year, he underwent a debilitating operation for stomach cancer. When I spoke with him in February, he was making plans to sing again, but his voice was practically a whisper. Still, he eagerly testified about &#x201C;Changes.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">&#x201C;Oh, my God,&#x201D; he said, &#x201C;when I heard the middle of the song, where it&#x2019;s, &#x2018;It took so long/To realize/That I can still hear/Her last goodbyes,&#x2019; that really touched my soul, truthfully. I used to think my mom was evil, but we were able to find forgiveness at the end of her life. Now I can go out into the world without animosity or anger and show people the love in my soul.&#x201D;&#x2666;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><em>Charles Aaron is a contributing writer for MTV News.</em></p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n          <div class=\"read-more\">Read more</div>\n        </div>\n      </section>\n      \n      <div class=\"m-ad-container\"></div>\n      \n    \n      <section id=\"/frank-ocean-seigfried\" data-id=\"frank-ocean-seigfried\"\n        class=\"m-section  frank-ocean\">\n        \n        <header class=\"m-header\">\n          <div class=\"m-header-meta\">\n            <h2 class=\"m-number\">23<span class=\"dot\">.<span></h2>\n            \n            <h2 class=\"m-headline\">\n              <span class=\"m-player\" data-slug=\"frank-ocean-seigfried\">\n                <audio class=\"m-audio\" src=\"https://p.scdn.co/mp3-preview/760643d03f5c24d96c159a3a52bc6d612cc63390?cid=null\"></audio>\n              </span>\n              <span class=\"m-headline-text\">Seigfried</span>\n            </h2>\n            <h4 class=\"m-artist\">Frank Ocean</h4>\n            \n            <div class=\"m-header-footer\">\n              <p class=\"m-leadin\">On the boldness and cowardice of love.</p>\n              <p class=\"m-byline\">By JASON PARHAM</p>\n            </div>\n            <div class=\"tools-wrapper\">\n              <div class=\"m-sharetools\" data-shares=\"facebook,twitter\"\n                data-url=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2017/03/09/magazine/25-songs-that-tell-us-where-music-is-going.html#/frank-ocean-seigfried\"\n                data-title=\"What Frank Ocean's work can tell us about where music is headed.\"\n                data-description=\"\"></div>\n              \n              <a class=\"m-full-track-link\" href=\"https://open.spotify.com/track/1BViPjTT585XAhkUUrkts0\" target=\"_blank\">\n                <img class=\"spotify-icon\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2017/03/03/music2017/assets/icons/spotify.svg\" />Full Track\n              </a>\n              \n            </div>\n            \n          </div>\n        </header>\n        \n        <div class=\"content \">\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Five years ago, on a sticky July afternoon, I stood in the kitchen of a friend&#x2019;s apartment, face jammed to an iridescent iPhone screen, poring over Frank Ocean&#x2019;s &#x201C;coming out letter.&#x201D; What seemed radical then now feels so achingly true to Ocean&#x2019;s uncategorizable spirit: He&#x2019;d posted <a href=\"http://frankocean.tumblr.com/post/26473798723\">a screen shot</a> of a TextEdit file to his personal Tumblr page and, in the echo of a Lucille Clifton or Countee Cullen poem, detailed his summers-long relationship with another man, his first love. Five years later, one passage in it has yet to unhook itself from me: &#x201C;HUMAN BEINGS SPINNING ON BLACKNESS. ALL WANTING TO BE SEEN, TOUCHED, HEARD, PAID ATTENTION TO.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Whether you have the emotional bandwidth to admit it or not, all of us, at some point in our volatile existence on Earth, want to be acknowledged by another human being &#x2014; seen, touched, heard and paid attention to, and not just platonically. Maybe those two lines jolted me because I live in an ebb-and-flow of denial about my own romantic relationships. Of the four semi-serious ones I&#x2019;ve had, not one has lasted longer than a year. (Once I asked an ex, with whom I&#x2019;d remained on good terms, to send me a favorite sentence of hers for a collaborative writing project I was working on; maybe half-jokingly, but probably not, she responded with the St. Vincent lyric &#x201C;Your heart is a strange little orange to peel.&#x201D;) And if music, like all art, should be an exercise in just how honest you are willing to be with yourself, then here are two truths: One is that I have never once known the texture of love &#x2014; its nirvana-inducing high, its valley of heartbreak. The other is the logical, dead-on explanation for this &#x2014; I am a coward. I refuse to take my guard down and open up.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Cowardice is also something you innately recognize in another. And on &#x201C;Seigfried,&#x201D; a moody, astral slow burner from Frank Ocean&#x2019;s &#x201C;Blonde,&#x201D; I see an ideal palette for Ocean to mask cowardice as love. Has self-confliction ever sounded this divine? Over a bed of drowned-out guitar chords, he sings: &#x201C;Maybe I&#x2019;m a fool/To settle for a place with some nice views/Maybe I should move/And settle down, two kids and a swimming pool/I&#x2019;m not brave.&#x201D; But it&#x2019;s the end of the song that finds Ocean sounding his most tormented. &#x201C;I&#x2019;d do anything for you,&#x201D; he croons in the final minute, pausing for a beat and then adding, &#x201C;in the dark.&#x201D; He repeats the line five or six times. It&#x2019;s such a pure exchange, and a beautiful one too. Feelings run up against one another: vulnerability and intimacy, the possibility of love. But there&#x2019;s also that undercurrent of cowardice. It&#x2019;s a deep, genuine promise with a built-in provision. I am yours, but only when the lights are low and no one is looking.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Perhaps it&#x2019;s not a surprising admission in a song that borrows from the singer-songwriter Elliott Smith, himself an effigy of melancholia, who died in 2003. But Ocean has always seemed so unafraid to me, both in his personal life and in the way his music emerges from the inside out. I suppose the bravery we see in him is evidenced by his very promise: though cloaked in a haze of emotional discord, it is one offered on his terms (Ocean has insisted on this position of self-authorship and ownership with increasing brazenness ever since the success of his first LP, &#x201C;Channel Orange&#x201D;). Maybe he has become more of the man he has always wanted to be in the years since the summer of 2012. I certainly haven&#x2019;t.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">The truth is, many of us are still &#x201C;spinning on blackness&#x201D; in our day-to-day lives &#x2014; at times lost, afraid, uncertain. But tonight, here in the dark and away from everybody else, Ocean&#x2019;s orchestral psalm is a reminder that you can still be in full control, even if that means retreating to a place you vowed never to return to. It is only with this kind of startling humanness &#x2014; wading through life&#x2019;s moral gray &#x2014; that Ocean could deliver such a promise. Because, really, this isn&#x2019;t about love or lust; it&#x2019;s about power. Harness it. Hold it close. And don&#x2019;t let go.&#x2666;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><em>Jason Parham is a senior editor for The Fader magazine.</em></p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n          <div class=\"read-more\">Read more</div>\n        </div>\n      </section>\n      \n    \n      <section id=\"/mitski-your-best-american-girl\" data-id=\"mitski-your-best-american-girl\"\n        class=\"m-section feature mitski\">\n        \n        <header class=\"m-header\">\n          <div class=\"m-header-meta\">\n            <h2 class=\"m-number\">24<span class=\"dot\">.<span></h2>\n            \n            <h2 class=\"m-headline\">\n              <span class=\"m-player\" data-slug=\"mitski-your-best-american-girl\">\n                <audio class=\"m-audio\" src=\"https://p.scdn.co/mp3-preview/9be3679be4314855260ca9010e4a6a73f74e4d28?cid=null\"></audio>\n              </span>\n              <span class=\"m-headline-text\">Your Best American Girl</span>\n            </h2>\n            \n            <div class=\"m-header-footer\">\n              <p class=\"m-leadin\">Growing up wondering if the American dream is for you.</p>\n              <p class=\"m-byline\">By JENNY ZHANG</p>\n            </div>\n            <div class=\"tools-wrapper\">\n              <div class=\"m-sharetools\" data-shares=\"facebook,twitter\"\n                data-url=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2017/03/09/magazine/25-songs-that-tell-us-where-music-is-going.html#/mitski-your-best-american-girl\"\n                data-title=\"What Mitski's work can tell us about where music is headed.\"\n                data-description=\"\"></div>\n              \n              <a class=\"m-full-track-link\" href=\"https://open.spotify.com/track/172rW45GEnGoJUuWfm1drt\" target=\"_blank\">\n                <img class=\"spotify-icon\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2017/03/03/music2017/assets/icons/spotify.svg\" />Full Track\n              </a>\n              \n            </div>\n            \n              <h4 class=\"m-artist\">Mitski</h4>\n            \n          </div>\n        </header>\n        \n        <div class=\"content \">\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><span class=\"dropcap\">G</span>rowing up in America, I experienced two puberties. The first opened me up to the possibilities of adulthood. The second reinforced that for someone like me &#x2014; an immigrant, a minority, an Asian-American &#x2014; there were limits. In this second coming-of-age, I had to contend with the pain of wanting a beautiful white body, not out of some misguided vanity, but because I saw over and over how whiteness conferred an instant legitimacy.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">So when the indie musician Mitski Miyawaki titled her fourth studio album &#x201C;Puberty 2,&#x201D; I felt an immediate flicker of recognition.
