I felt another when she released the first single, &#x201C;Your Best American Girl,&#x201D; last March. Coming from someone like Mitski, who was born in Japan to a Japanese mother and an American father, the title alone was powerful enough to reopen a wound that had been rotting inside me ever since I came of age as an Asian girl in America: an old hatred for myself, my culture, the way I looked and the way I was raised.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            \n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  <figure\n  id=\"media-100000004967582\"\n  class=\"media photo vertical jumbo\">\n  <div class=\"image\">\n    <img src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music3-MITSKI/12music-madigan2-master180.jpg\"\n    data-ratio=\"1.5\"\n    data-pattern=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music3-MITSKI/12music-madigan2-{{size}}.jpg\"\n    data-widths='[{\"slug\":\"blogSmallInline\",\"size\":151},{\"slug\":\"master180\",\"size\":180},{\"slug\":\"blog225\",\"size\":225},{\"slug\":\"slide\",\"size\":334},{\"slug\":\"popup\",\"size\":334},{\"slug\":\"tmagSF\",\"size\":362},{\"slug\":\"blog427\",\"size\":427},{\"slug\":\"blog480\",\"size\":480},{\"slug\":\"master495\",\"size\":495},{\"slug\":\"blog533\",\"size\":533},{\"slug\":\"tmagArticle\",\"size\":592},{\"slug\":\"articleLarge\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"master675\",\"size\":675},{\"slug\":\"jumbo\",\"size\":683},{\"slug\":\"master1050\",\"size\":1050},{\"slug\":\"superJumbo\",\"size\":1366}]'\n    itemprop=\"url\"/>\n  </div>\n  <figcaption class=\"caption\">\n    <span class=\"caption-text\"></span>\n    <span class=\"credit\">Erik Madigan Heck for The New York Times. Stylist: Bobette Cohn. Hair: Jordan M. Makeup: Aya Komatsu.</span>\n  </figcaption>\n</figure>\n\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">The simple bass line that opens the song should feel familiar to anyone who ever bobbed along with &#x2019;90s indie rock while indulging in self-pity (as I most certainly did). Mitski murmurs and sighs and sings about a lover. &#x201C;If I could, I&#x2019;d be your little spoon/And kiss your fingers forever more.&#x201D; Did it mean something to me to hear another Asian-American woman refer to herself in the diminutive? Of course it did, and even more to hear her sing, &#x201C;But big spoon, you have so much to do/And I have nothing ahead of me.&#x201D; The guitar and drum beat join in, adding bulk, while Mitski&#x2019;s voice remains at a steady, low ache, elevating him to the status of a god, unable to imagine that anything could be off-limits to him: &#x201C;You&#x2019;re the sun, you&#x2019;ve never seen the night.&#x201D; She denigrates herself again: &#x201C;Well, I&#x2019;m not the moon, I&#x2019;m not even a star.&#x201D; Then, after the power chords kick in and the chorus lifts off: &#x201C;Your mother wouldn&#x2019;t approve of how my mother raised me/But I do, I think I do.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Mothers have always held such symbolic weight in determining a person&#x2019;s worth. Your mother tongue, your motherland, your mother&#x2019;s values &#x2014; these things can qualify or disqualify you from attaining myriad American dreams: love, fluency, citizenship, legitimacy, acceptance, success, freedom. &#x201C;You&#x2019;re an all-American boy,&#x201D; Mitski sings. &#x201C;I guess I couldn&#x2019;t help trying to be your best American girl.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">I wanted to hear Mitski&#x2019;s story in this song, but I only heard my own. Listening brought me back to the fuzzy &#x2019;90s D.I.Y. scene of my adolescence in the suburbs of Long Island, back when no one much questioned why a subculture that saw itself as rebelling against the establishment was quite so dominated by white men. I grew up in that scene, as far as someone like me could &#x2014; a self-hating Asian kid with strict immigrant parents who wouldn&#x2019;t let me go over to white people&#x2019;s houses for fear that their values (laziness, selfishness, drug abuse, sexual promiscuity) would infect me. My mother had two unshakable beliefs that she tried to drill into me. The first was that I had to study and work twice as hard as my white peers if I wanted to survive in America, and the second was that it was delusional and dangerous to believe I possessed the same freedom white people had to pursue my dreams.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">We never actually said &#x201C;white people&#x201D;; we used the Chinese term for &#x201C;American,&#x201D; <em>mei guo ren</em>. But it was clear which Americans we were referring to. For over a decade, she and my father worked two and sometimes three jobs, on top of night classes, until they saved enough to move us into an upper-middle-class, mostly white neighborhood. &#x201C;You&#x2019;ve trapped me,&#x201D; I used to argue to her in high school. &#x201C;You brought me to this place but you won&#x2019;t let me be a part of it.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">When I told my mother I wanted to be a writer, she reacted as if I had said I wanted to kill myself. She vowed that she would do everything humanly possible to stop me. Compared with how she had behaved as a teenager, I was ungrateful; compared with the white mothers I knew, she was a dictator. At her most cutting, she asked me, &#x201C;Who&#x2019;s ever going to read your stories?&#x201D; I was stumped. I couldn&#x2019;t say. At that point in my life, kids were still shouting my last name back to me in the hallways as if it were a punch line and asking me if Chinese people really ate dogs. For the first time, it occurred to me that maybe my mother wasn&#x2019;t trying to stop me so much as she was trying to protect me.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            \n            <figure\n  id=\"graphic-100000004976566\"\n  class=\"media graphic size-300\"\n  data-minimum-width=\"300\" >\n  <div class=\"interactive interactive-embedded\"\n       style=\"max-width: 300px\">\n    \n    <figcaption class=\"interactive-caption\">\n      <h2 class=\"interactive-headline\"></h2>\n      <h4 class=\"interactive-summary\"></h4>\n    </figcaption>\n    \n    <div class=\"interactive-graphic\"><script>function getFlexData() { return {\"data\":{\"quote\":\"My mother believed that being an outsider here was a finer fate and home than any other she could have had.\",\"attribution\":\"\"}}; }var NYTD=NYTD || {}; NYTD.FlexTypes = NYTD.FlexTypes || []; NYTD.FlexTypes.push({\"target\":\"FT100000004976566\",\"type\":\"Pull Quote\",\"data\":{\"quote\":\"My mother believed that being an outsider here was a finer fate and home than any other she could have had.\",\"attribution\":\"\"}});</script><link href=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2015/02/10/mag-pullquote-redesign/assets/style.css\" type=\"text/css\" rel=\"stylesheet\">\r\n\r\n<script>\r\n  var NYTG_ASSETS = \"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2015/02/10/mag-pullquote-redesign/b585ac490ed3103c57423bc4f581ffac30ebc5df/\",\r\n      NYTG_BIG_ASSETS = \"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2015/02/10/mag-pullquote-redesign/assets/\",\r\n      _gaq = _gaq || [];\r\n  require(['foundation/main'], function() {\r\n    require([\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2015/02/10/mag-pullquote-redesign/assets/build.js\"]); // generated from src/script.js\r\n  });\r\n</script><div id=\"FT100000004976566\"></div></div>\n    <div class=\"footer\">\n      <div class=\"interactive-notes\"></div>\n      <div class=\"interactive-source\"></div>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</figure>\n\n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">There&#x2019;s a line in the second verse of &#x201C;Your Best American Girl&#x201D;: &#x201C;You&#x2019;re the one/You&#x2019;re all I ever wanted/I think I&#x2019;ll regret this.&#x201D; It hints at what so many nonwhite Americans have long suspected: that our desire to be loved may be truly masochistic. It goes unquestioned that the non-American girl would want to assimilate into the all-American boy&#x2019;s world. But she cannot. That world is not for her.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Racism thrives when the people being harmed by it blame themselves and one another. As a teenager, I blamed my parents for failing to secure me admission into whiteness, which I was certain was a prerequisite to being loved. I was mad at them, not at the cruelty of the American dream or the ways in which white supremacy had warped each of us. My privileged upbringing and education and linguistic fluency gave me such proximity to whiteness that it stung all the more to still find myself outside of it. My mother, on the other hand, not only accepted that she would always be an outsider in this country but also believed it to be a finer fate and home than any other she could have had.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">As I grow older, I find myself trying to turn to her more, to ask the questions I never asked when I was too busy feeling sorry for myself: why she came here, what it was like to uproot a life and move across the world, whether it was hard to raise me here. Does Mitski&#x2019;s narrator really approve of how her mother raised her? It&#x2019;s unclear, but you can hear in her voice just how much she wants to.&#x2666;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><em>Jenny Zhang is the author of the forthcoming short-story collection &#x201C;Sour Heart.&#x201D;</em></p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n          <div class=\"read-more\">Read more</div>\n        </div>\n      </section>\n      \n      <div class=\"m-ad-container\"></div>\n      \n    \n      <section id=\"/migos-bad-and-boujee\" data-id=\"migos-bad-and-boujee\"\n        class=\"m-section  migos\">\n        \n        <header class=\"m-header\">\n          <div class=\"m-header-meta\">\n            <h2 class=\"m-number\">25<span class=\"dot\">.<span></h2>\n            \n            <h2 class=\"m-headline\">\n              <span class=\"m-player\" data-slug=\"migos-bad-and-boujee\">\n                <audio class=\"m-audio\" src=\"https://p.scdn.co/mp3-preview/7692f502539573458844535e54f247643d2f7157?cid=null\"></audio>\n              </span>\n              <span class=\"m-headline-text\">Bad and Boujee</span>\n            </h2>\n            <h4 class=\"m-artist\">Migos</h4>\n            \n            <div class=\"m-header-footer\">\n              <p class=\"m-leadin\">Raindrop. Drop top. Is rap music the new punk rock?</p>\n              <p class=\"m-byline\">By NAOMI ZEICHNER</p>\n            </div>\n            <div class=\"tools-wrapper\">\n              <div class=\"m-sharetools\" data-shares=\"facebook,twitter\"\n                data-url=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2017/03/09/magazine/25-songs-that-tell-us-where-music-is-going.html#/migos-bad-and-boujee\"\n                data-title=\"What Migos's work can tell us about where music is headed.\"\n                data-description=\"\"></div>\n              \n              <a class=\"m-full-track-link\" href=\"https://open.spotify.com/track/4Km5HrUvYTaSUfiSGPJeQR\" target=\"_blank\">\n                <img class=\"spotify-icon\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2017/03/03/music2017/assets/icons/spotify.svg\" />Full Track\n              </a>\n              \n            </div>\n            \n          </div>\n        </header>\n        \n        <div class=\"content \">\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Ever since the election in November, I&#x2019;ve gone out more and stayed out later. This doesn&#x2019;t feel glamorous or especially healthy. People who go to clubs regularly are usually there to work &#x2014; whether they make or manage or write about music or just labor to look great. You spend more money than you have and try not to think about it, because late at night, positive energy is the most valuable currency. If anything made the hangover the next morning worth the trouble, it was Migos&#x2019;s &#x201C;Bad and Boujee,&#x201D; which dominated parties of all kinds this freakishly warm winter.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">The Atlanta rap trio &#x2014; made up of the rappers Quavo, Offset and Takeoff &#x2014; have been around for years, but &#x201C;Bad and Boujee&#x201D; represents their most significant foray into the mainstream, and it&#x2019;s still a slightly awkward fit. For a recent music-video shoot, they wore heavy-metal tees and studded leather; Quavo&#x2019;s jacket was painted with the skull logo of the Misfits. This is an increasingly common trope in rap these days; many hip-hop artists insist they are rock stars to signal that they are at the center of today&#x2019;s counterculture &#x2014; which is true. The trio records constantly and has worked the live circuit hard. First the rappers conquered Atlanta&#x2019;s strip-club scene, then the V.I.P. booths of the surrounding states and then the money of corporations. On their recent album &#x201C;Culture,&#x201D; Quavo reflects on their lifestyle: &#x201C;All this pain, we can live through it. It&#x2019;s called success.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            \n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  <figure\n  id=\"media-100000004967830\"\n  class=\"media photo horizontal jumbo\">\n  <div class=\"image\">\n    <img src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music12-migos/12music-MIGOS-master180.jpg\"\n    data-ratio=\"0.75\"\n    data-pattern=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12music12-migos/12music-MIGOS-{{size}}.jpg\"\n    data-widths='[{\"slug\":\"blogSmallInline\",\"size\":151},{\"slug\":\"master180\",\"size\":180},{\"slug\":\"blog225\",\"size\":225},{\"slug\":\"tmagSF\",\"size\":362},{\"slug\":\"blog427\",\"size\":427},{\"slug\":\"blog480\",\"size\":480},{\"slug\":\"master495\",\"size\":495},{\"slug\":\"blog533\",\"size\":533},{\"slug\":\"tmagArticle\",\"size\":592},{\"slug\":\"articleLarge\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"slide\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"popup\",\"size\":650},{\"slug\":\"master675\",\"size\":675},{\"slug\":\"jumbo\",\"size\":1024},{\"slug\":\"master1050\",\"size\":1050},{\"slug\":\"superJumbo\",\"size\":2048}]'\n    itemprop=\"url\"/>\n  </div>\n  <figcaption class=\"caption\">\n    <span class=\"caption-text\"></span>\n    <span class=\"credit\">Chad Batka</span>\n  </figcaption>\n</figure>\n\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">&#x201C;Culture&#x201D; triumphed on the back of &#x201C;Bad and Boujee,&#x201D; which hit No. 1 on the Billboard Hot 100 chart in January after months of gestation, thanks in part to internet memes that parroted back its most memorable parts. Migos is known for building songs around catchy lines, and &#x201C;Bad and Boujee&#x201D; is expertly crammed with them, from its opening preamble to its abstract poetic &#x201C;raindrop, drop top&#x201D; hook and the five ecstatic woos that announce the beginning of Offset&#x2019;s verse. The song is nearly six minutes long, and these noises are like souvenirs collected by everyone who has traveled through it, a trip that usually happens at the pinnacle of a night out, when the dance floor is packed and the women in the crowd are ready. &#x201C;You can just tell,&#x201D; one D.J. friend said, when I asked how he decides when to play it. &#x201C;It&#x2019;s almost as if someone whispers in your ear, &#x2018;It&#x2019;s time.&#x2019; &#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">With all its parts to latch onto, &#x201C;Bad and Boujee&#x201D; draws people together. When its bass shakes, people do, too; you could cut the music off and everyone would still be yelling in unison. Surrounded by that kind of collective precision, you might find it difficult not to feel less alone. If Migos has to pose in punk-rock outfits to be recognized as a voice for its generation, at least it&#x2019;s an authentic pose: The punks I know love their music deeply. Whether they meant to or not, with &#x201C;Bad and Boujee,&#x201D; Migos created a space of refuge for people who needed a break and were willing to work for it. The song offered a sanctuary: Settle in, stay a while, belong here for as long as you need.&#x2666;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><em>Naomi Zeichner is the editor in chief of The Fader.</em></p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n          <div class=\"read-more\">Read more</div>\n        </div>\n      </section>\n      \n    \n      <section id=\"/metallica-spit-out-the-bone\" data-id=\"metallica-spit-out-the-bone\"\n        class=\"m-section  metallica\">\n        \n        <header class=\"m-header\">\n          <div class=\"m-header-meta\">\n            <h2 class=\"m-number\">BONUS TRACK<span class=\"dot\">.<span></h2>\n            \n            <h2 class=\"m-headline\">\n              <span class=\"m-player\" data-slug=\"metallica-spit-out-the-bone\">\n                <audio class=\"m-audio\" src=\"https://p.scdn.co/mp3-preview/cd6c5d07ff65e4a7ed4dcd3765bb83727d989211?cid=null\"></audio>\n              </span>\n              <span class=\"m-headline-text\">Spit Out the Bone</span>\n            </h2>\n            <h4 class=\"m-artist\">Metallica</h4>\n            \n            <div class=\"m-header-footer\">\n              <p class=\"m-leadin\">You play you.</p>\n              <p class=\"m-byline\">By Steven Hyden</p>\n            </div>\n            <div class=\"tools-wrapper\">\n              <div class=\"m-sharetools\" data-shares=\"facebook,twitter\"\n                data-url=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2017/03/09/magazine/25-songs-that-tell-us-where-music-is-going.html#/metallica-spit-out-the-bone\"\n                data-title=\"What Metallica's work can tell us about where music is headed.\"\n                data-description=\"\"></div>\n              \n              <a class=\"m-full-track-link\" href=\"https://open.spotify.com/track/7MHkweinOceprzkRWIYTTc\" target=\"_blank\">\n                <img class=\"spotify-icon\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2017/03/03/music2017/assets/icons/spotify.svg\" />Full Track\n              </a>\n              \n            </div>\n            \n          </div>\n        </header>\n        \n        <div class=\"content \">\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Last November, Metallica did something extraordinary: It put out a new album, &#x201C;Hardwired .&#x2009;.&#x2009;. to Self-Destruct,&#x201D; that actually sounded like Metallica. On paper, a band acting like itself seems normal. But consider Metallica&#x2019;s output since its megaselling self-titled LP in 1991, better known as &#x201C;The Black Album.&#x201D; There was the &#x201C;alt-rock haircut&#x201D; phase of &#x201C;Load and Re-Load.&#x201D; The &#x201C;Let&#x2019;s stop playing guitar solos&#x201D; phase of &#x201C;St. Anger.&#x201D; And, most regrettable, the &#x201C;Collaborate with Lou Reed on a confounding art-rock opus about a 19th-century German femme fatale&#x201D; phase of &#x201C;Lulu.&#x201D;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Contrast those digressions with &#x201C;Spit Out the Bone,&#x201D; the kinetic seven-minute closer of &#x201C;Hardwired.&#x201D; Dueling riffs by the guitarists James Hetfield and Kirk Hammett race forward at a frightening velocity. Lars Ulrich bashes out a martial beat that sounds like a velociraptor trapped inside a washing machine. And Robert Trujillo&#x2019;s melodically sinister bass snakes below the maelstrom, a demon lurking in the shadows. &#x201C;Spit Out the Bone&#x201D; epitomizes the thrash-metal formula that Metallica helped invent in the early &#x2019;80s and then tried to avoid for the past quarter-century. On &#x201C;Hardwired ... to Self-Destruct,&#x201D; it&#x2019;s almost as if those 25 years never happened.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            \n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  <figure\n  id=\"media-100000004973612\"\n  class=\"media photo horizontal jumbo\">\n  <div class=\"image\">\n    <img src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12endpaper/12endpaper-master180.jpg\"\n    data-ratio=\"0.6666666666666666\"\n    data-pattern=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2017/03/12/magazine/12endpaper/12endpaper-{{size}}.jpg\"\n    data-widths='[{\"slug\":\"blogSmallInline\",\"size\":151},{\"slug\":\"master180\",\"size\":180},{\"slug\":\"blog225\",\"size\":225},{\"slug\":\"tmagSF\",\"size\":362},{\"slug\":\"blog427\",\"size\":427},{\"slug\":\"blog480\",\"size\":480},{\"slug\":\"master495\",\"size\":495},{\"slug\":\"blog533\",\"size\":533},{\"slug\":\"tmagArticle\",\"size\":592},{\"slug\":\"articleLarge\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"slide\",\"size\":600},{\"slug\":\"popup\",\"size\":650},{\"slug\":\"master675\",\"size\":675},{\"slug\":\"jumbo\",\"size\":1024},{\"slug\":\"master1050\",\"size\":1050},{\"slug\":\"superJumbo\",\"size\":1750}]'\n    itemprop=\"url\"/>\n  </div>\n  <figcaption class=\"caption\">\n    <span class=\"caption-text\"></span>\n    <span class=\"credit\">Illustration by Erik Carter</span>\n  </figcaption>\n</figure>\n\n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Not giving the people what they want has been admired as a gesture of artistic fortitude in music, going back at least to Igor Stravinsky, whose premiere of his dissonant masterwork, &#x201C;The Rite of Spring,&#x201D; caused a riot at the Th&#xE9;&#xE2;tre des Champs-&#xC9;lys&#xE9;es in Paris in 1913. More recently, a boxed set collecting every known concert recording from Bob Dylan&#x2019;s 1966 tour &#x2014; 36 discs in all &#x2014; was released last fall. The exhaustive document chronicles a pivotal period for rock&#x2019;s poet laureate, who angered folk fans by &#x201C;going electric.&#x201D; Now it&#x2019;s possible to hear nearly every instance of an audience jeering loudly as Dylan introduced &#x201C;Like a Rolling Stone&#x201D; and other songs into his live repertoire.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Metallica once embraced a foundational idea of &#x201C;progressive&#x201D; popular music: Change constantly or become instantly irrelevant, even if those stylistic shifts alienate your fans. But in an age when a plurality of niches has supplanted the mainstream that Metallica once conquered, there&#x2019;s a new kind of street cred that comes with sticking to what you&#x2019;re known for. Now it&#x2019;s listeners who contain multitudes. It&#x2019;s up to us to create eclectic playlists designed for a variety of moods and situations. For a veteran band like Metallica, that means being rewarded for playing to type. &#x201C;Hardwired ... to Self-Destruct&#x201D; debuted at No.&#x2009;1 and was the best-selling rock album in years.</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\">Is it antithetical to art to suggest that &#x201C;know your brand&#x201D; is the future not just of music, but also all of entertainment? George Lucas sold the &#x201C;Star Wars&#x201D; franchise to Disney in 2012, and now J.&#x2009;J. Abrams makes the sort of &#x201C;fun&#x201D; &#x201C;Star Wars&#x201D; movies that Lucas was trying to get away from with his little-loved, self-serious prequels. Lucas never recovered after making his versions of &#x201C;Load,&#x201D; &#x201C;St. Anger&#x201D; and &#x201C;Lulu.&#x201D; Metallica should be grateful that it still gets to make Metallica records.&#x2666;</p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n            <p class=\"paragraph\"><em>Steven Hyden is cultural critic for Uproxx.</em></p>\n            \n\t\t\t\t\t  \n          \n          <div class=\"read-more\">Read more</div>\n        </div>\n      </section>\n      \n      <div class=\"m-ad-container\"></div>\n      \n    \n      <section id=\"/the-ep-the-music-issue-podcast\" class=\"m-number\">BONUS TRACK<span class=\"dot\">.<span></h2>\n            \n            <h2 class=\"m-headline\">\n              <span class=\"m-player\" data-slug=\"the-ep-the-music-issue-podcast\">\n                <audio class=\"m-audio\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/newsgraphics/2017/03/03/music2017/assets/audio/nytmag_ep_2017.mp3\"></audio>\n              </span>\n              <span class=\"m-headline-text\">The Music Issue Podcast</span>\n            </h2>\n            \n            <div class=\"m-header-footer\">\n              <p class=\"m-leadin\">We talk with some of the great writers in this issue about the songs that spoke to them over the past year, and what those songs might tell us about the world right now.</p>\n                                    <h3 class=\"kicker\">The Music Issue</h3>\n                        <h2 class=\"story-heading\">25 Songs That Tell Us Where Music Is Going</h2>\n            <time class=\"dateline\" datetime=\"2016-03-10\" itemprop=\"dateModified\" content=\"2016-03-10\">MARCH 10, 2016</time>\n                    </a>\n    </article>\n</li>\n \n                            <li>\n    <article class=\"story theme-summary \">\n        <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2016/12/08/magazine/great-performers-la-noir-the-years-most-captivating-film-performances.html\">\n                              <h3 class=\"kicker\">Feature</h3>\n                        <h2 class=\"story-heading\">The Dirty Projectors Go Solo</h2>\n            <time class=\"dateline\"     </div><!-- close page -->\n    </div><!-- close shell -->\n    <script>\nrequire(['foundation/main'], function () {\n    require(['interactive/main']);\n    require(['jquery/nyt', 'foundation/views/page-manager'], function ($, pageManager) {\n        if (window.location.search.indexOf('disable_tagx') > 0) {\n            return;\n        }\n        $(document).ready(function () {\n            require(['https://a1.nyt.com/analytics/tagx-simple.min.js'], function () {\n                pageManager.trackingFireEventQueue();\n            });\n        });\n    });\n});\n</script>\n    <div id=\"Inv1\" class=\"ad inv1-ad hidden\"></div>\n<div id=\"Inv2\" class=\"ad inv2-ad hidden\"></div>\n<div id=\"Inv3\" class=\"ad inv3-ad hidden\"></div>\n<div id=\"ab1\" class=\"ad ab1-ad hidden\"></div>\n<div id=\"ab2\" class=\"ad ab2-ad hidden\"></div>\n<div id=\"ab3\" class=\"ad ab3-ad hidden\"></div>\n<div id=\"prop1\" class=\"ad prop1-ad hidden\"></div>\n<div id=\"prop2\" class=\"ad prop2-ad hidden\"></div>\n<div id=\"Anchor\" class=\"ad anchor-ad hidden\"></div>\n<div id=\"ADX_CLIENTSIDE\" class=\"ad adx-clientside-ad hidden\"></div>\n</body>\n</html>\n", "title": "25 Songs That Tell Us Where Music Is Going", "url": "http://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2017/03/09/magazine/25-songs-that-tell-us-where-music-is-going.html?src=rec&amp;recp=4", "date": "03-10-2017", "source": "new-york-times", "text": "The 33-year-old musician Kelela favors the kind of fashion aesthetic that science-fiction films sometimes use to signify characters from the future: gravity-defying materials in iridescent or metallic colors. For a recent rainy night in Strasbourg, the small city in the northeastern corner of France, she strode onstage dressed like a lieutenant in an anime cartoon, in an oversize gray bomber jacket, matching shorts and heels made from white fabric that stretched above her knees. She raised her hands and gave a hard stare to the crowd. \u201cMy mission tonight,\u201d she said, \u201cis to show the breadth of R.&B. music. It has influenced every genre, pretty much, so anyone who thinks it is basic or rudimentary has another thing coming.\u201d\n\nThere were no whoops, claps or even smiles. The audience remained passive. Kelela likes to keep an eye out for the edges of the crowd, where her core fans (\u201cthe queer black and brown weirdos\u201d as she put it to me) usually congregate. But tonight, the scene was homogeneous in a very European way: Women favored striped boatnecks, red lips and messy topknots; the men, zipped-up pullovers and spotless white trainers. Kelela nodded at her D.J., Loric Sih, a sweet-faced boy with bleached blond hair and wire-rimmed Harry Potter glasses, and they dove into her set. True to her word, amid the switchbacks of her feathery falsetto voice, there was no mistaking the roots of classic R.&B. \u2014 all set to spacey electronic beats far outside the traditional canon. The room became a sound installation of Kelela\u2019s reverb-y vocals and synthetic \u201990s-era Miami bass.\n\nKelela\u2019s stage was minimally adorned, but her lighting team is adept at creating James Turrell-like lightscapes that drape her figure in rich reds, purples and blues. At one point, her face and body were illuminated by an electric shade of cyan, while the background remained shaded in dark azure. The effect made Kelela look as ethereal and spectral as the music radiating from the speakers. The handful of times I\u2019d seen her perform in the United States, the audience was rapt for the entire performance \u2014 reverent during her atmospheric songs, breaking into exuberant, feverish dance during her fast-paced ones. (Her music can keep the lovesick company in bed just as easily as it can shepherd a party past sunrise.)\n\nBut that night the concertgoers remained inscrutable. When she transitioned into a new song \u2014 \u201cBlue Light,\u201d the first single from her long-awaited debut album \u2014 I pulled out my phone and sent the recording to some friends back home. Some 4,000 miles away, they seemed more excited than the people physically present in the concert hall. Finally, about 30 minutes into her set, Sih began playing \u201cRewind,\u201d the closest thing Kelela has to a pop song. The audience, charmed at last, succumbed to the irresistible beat and danced along. The moment was buoyant but short-lived: It was her last number. She thanked the crowd and then bounded offstage.\n\nWhen she was back in her dressing room, the composure Kelela had projected to the audience quickly dissipated. She stood with her hands on her hips, chewing on her lip. Her boyfriend \u2014 a filmmaker named Cieron Magat, with whom she shares an apartment in London \u2014 murmured words of reassurance and handed her a cup of homemade ginger tea. \u201cThat was one of the worst ones,\u201d she said, sighing and taking off her earrings. Magat told her not to worry, but Kelela wanted to deconstruct the performance.\n\n\u201cThe thing I\u2019m always looking for are the eyes, or even the face that\u2019s like, I don\u2019t know what this is but I\u2019m into it,\u201d she said. \u201cBut I got nothing.\u201d She peeled her clothes off absent-mindedly and paced around her dressing room. \u201cI was this guy\u201d \u2014 she threw her arms up in imitation of the shruggie emoticon \u2014 and sighed again. \u201cBut it\u2019s unrealistic in this context.\u201d\n\nBy context, Kelela meant that she wasn\u2019t the headliner \u2014 most people were there to see the main act, the moody British band the xx. Earlier in the day, while roaming around Strasbourg, I noticed that the posters advertising the show didn\u2019t even mention her name. That night, in the nearly sold-out venue, a space that could hold 4,000 people, only a few attendees were black; her \u201cqueer black and brown weirdos\u201d were missing. In the United States, Kelela is part of the vanguard of black female musicians who make emotional soul, women like Solange, SZA and Syd tha Kyd. The music of these women is aimed squarely at the heart chakra of young black women; it legitimizes as much as it asserts the value of being yourself \u2014 even if that self is thought to be a little off-center. Kelela, in particular, explodes the notion that blackness is monolithic, a single Pantone square instead of untold variations. Her music is geared to a generation that lives for juxtapositions and unexpected arrangements, sonically and visually.\n\nIn 2012, Kelela was performing at a show that Solange\u2019s manager happened to see. She asked for a demo and gave the song to Solange, who asked Kelela to come on tour with her later that year, introducing Kelela to an audience who could appreciate her innovations in R.&B. In October 2013, Kelela released \u201cCut 4 Me,\u201d an impressive mixtape composed of 13 songs that were initially free online. At the time, Kelela wanted to see how far she could push herself as an artist and play with the boundaries of R.&B. Kelela\u2019s uninhibited experimentation, as well as the rich latticework on songs like \u201cSend Me Out,\u201d impressed critics. Pitchfork gave the collection a rare 8.3 rating out of 10 and said she had \u201cthe talent to make herself heard, and the intelligence to get it all together\u201d; Spin called the collection \u201cstunning\u201d and said the singer could \u201cgo anywhere from here.\u201d That November, Solange chose two of Kelela\u2019s songs for \u201cSaint Heron,\u201d a compilation album released by Solange\u2019s label of the same name. In 2015, she released a six-song EP called \u201cHallucinogen.\u201d Her sound on the EP somehow managed to evoke Jimmy Jam and Terry Lewis, Bj\u00f6rk and Donna Summer all at the same time. It felt like a sonic relic of the past unearthed 100 years in the future. Since then, fans have been waiting for her first full-length album, which Kelela expects to release this year.\n\nIn her dressing room, Kelela folded herself into a pretzel on the couch next to me. A candle burned in the background. She knew it had been an off night, but because she loves performing so much, she was still buzzing from the energy. \u201cThe goal,\u201d she explained, \u201cis just \u2014 how many people can I put on, just so they can name it, even if they don\u2019t know what it is yet?\u201d\n\nKelela Mizanekristos was born in 1983 to Mizanekristos Yohannes and Neghist Girma, students who escaped war-torn Ethopia and immigrated separately to the United States. She was raised in Gaithersburg, Md. Kelela\u2019s parents introduced her to the violin when she was a child, and she practiced singing along to the radio in her bedroom at night and composing medleys in her head. Her father was fond of Blues Alley, an all-ages jazz supper club in Georgetown frequented by Dizzy Gillespie and Sarah Vaughan. He often took Kelela with him, and she fell in love with the culture of music. She listened to Kirk Franklin on the radio and learned to sing in ge\u2019ez, an ancient language used primarily in the Ethiopian church, which she attended with her mother. You can still catch the influence in her voice \u2014 the way she turns sounds into sacred geometry, almost unconsciously stairstepping through the vowels and consonants.\n\nIn her early to mid 20s, she would go to a Washington bar called 18th Street Lounge for its Sunday-night house sessions. A D.J. named Sam Burns played eclectic soul and deep house music, and after a few drinks, if she heard a bit of music that reminded her of another song, she would jump on the microphone and blend the two in real-time. \u201cI would run to the microphone and figure out a way to sing it. I would create a flip,\u201d she says. \u201cThat is where I live.\u201d\n\nHer first boyfriend, Kris Funn, whom she met when she was 19, played the upright bass, and she sat in bars for hours, watching him and his friends play. She joked to me that she was a \u201cjazz wife\u201d but also admitted that she received an unexpected education: She learned to listen to music, to get a feel for it. Eventually the couple broke up, but Funn encouraged Kelela to trust her instincts and not be intimidated by her lack of formal music training.\n\nBy that time, Kelela was a student at American University, studying international studies and sociology. \u201cI was the only brown girl, all the time, talking about African politics,\u201d she told me. \u201cI thought I was going to be an academic. In my head, I am supposed to be a college graduate. I wanted to finish. But I was not motivated to sit there and do that paper. I had a lot of resistance.\u201d She felt alienated by the program. She dropped out.\n\nThis was in 2006, and synthpop, epitomized by bands like the Knife, was trending. She began recording in a punk house in Washington, a city with a hard-core lineage that included acts like Fugazi and Bad Brains. She thrived in an environment devoid of rules. \u201cWhat\u2019s so beautiful about punk culture,\u201d Kelela says, \u201cis that the whole idea is that you don\u2019t have to be perfect. Just try. Let\u2019s just try to make it ourselves.\u201d At first she sang over indie rock, but it didn\u2019t feel authentic to her. She wanted to experiment with electronic music \u2014 \u201cnot real instruments,\u201d she says.\n\nShe spent hours on MySpace, scrolling through pages of music and listening to instrumentals. She recorded herself singing over sounds she liked. Then she would send the artist her sample, along with an invitation to collaborate. Two notable electronic producers agreed, including Daedelus, who featured her on a track. At the same time, a friend introduced her to the electro duo Teengirl Fantasy, and they created a song. Will Boston, a founder of the music label Fade to Mind, heard their collaboration and was impressed by what he described to me as her \u201cwealth of startling honesty, felt through her vocals.\u201d\n\nBy then, Kelela was living in Los Angeles, and Boston brought her a thumb drive of sounds from the label and its British counterpart, Night Slugs. Kelela spent the next several days poring over the files, improvising lyrics over the sounds she liked, turning them into songs. She loved the otherworldliness of the instrumentals \u2014 staccato mixes that used sound effects like tinkling glass and guns reloading over drum machines. The music complemented the gossamer scales she likes to sing in. It was \u201cexactly what I\u2019d been looking for,\u201d she says. Two of the songs she produced during this time were on the mixtape she released in 2013. Kelela took care to describe her role in developing the mixtape, to make certain I knew how active she was in it \u2014 perhaps to counter the idea that she isn\u2019t self-made or in charge of her own sound. \u201cThe first thing people want to take away from me is my agency,\u201d she told me.\n\nAlex Sushon, an electronic producer who goes by Bok Bok, was one of the artists Kelela met as she was working on her mixtape, and these days, she tends to work mostly with British producers like him, possibly because they\u2019ve been pushing the boundaries of R.&B. more aggressively by blending it with grime, the East London style of dance music. Electronica, Sushon told me, is referential in the same way that R.&B. tends to be. \u201cThat\u2019s how we think about music, inserting small samples from other genres, little shortcuts that are saying something by playing something,\u201d he says. Because of the internet, he explained, musicians can share references more easily than they did in the past. Google, YouTube and SoundCloud make it easy. Sushon compares this dynamic to \u201cmemes, but in musical form.\u201d\n\nThe closest analogy is sampling, but it\u2019s more complex than that. I watched Kelela and her D.J. perform this trick onstage, during one of their songs in Strasbourg. While she was singing \u201cThe High,\u201d a fuzzed-out, gritty ode to desire, Sih mixed in a few seconds of a 2002 song by Tweet called \u201cOops (Oh My),\u201d and Kelela segued into its vocals. Here, suddenly, was the thrilling flicker of a decade-old hit that had almost entirely faded from popular culture, tucked into her own noir love song. It was the perfect encapsulation of Kelela\u2019s odd 21st-century gift for taking familiar sounds and repurposing them in a new context, a kind of lyrical d\u00e9j\u00e0 vu.\n\nAfter the show, back at her Strasbourg Airbnb, Kelela changed into oversize gray sweatpants and a black button-down crop top, and padded into the kitchen in white slippers. She plugged in an electric kettle and made another cup of ginger tea as our conversation turned to her debut album. I expected her to talk about its sound, but she wanted to speak about the intention behind it. She described the album as \u201csomething that speaks to power.\u201d When I asked her to elaborate, she paused, then said: \u201cI\u2019m not talking to black people, to be honest. I don\u2019t need to tell black people that R.&B. is deep. It\u2019s about the music industry. When I say the white gaze, I\u2019m not just talking about white people at my show. I like that. I like playing to mixed crowds. For me, it\u2019s really just the way I\u2019m treated by people of positions of power who seemingly hold the key.\u201d\n\nThe dynamics that bothered Kelela in college didn\u2019t disappear when she became an artist \u2014 if anything, they intensified. As a student, she was introduced to black academics and feminists like Audre Lorde, Bell Hooks and Angela Davis. These women helped her make sense of the racial and sexist forces that shape the world, and she still turns to them to navigate the music industry. She internalized their insistence to not be apologetic for her womanhood or blackness and not be debilitated by exclusion.\n\nSprawled on the bed in the apartment\u2019s master bedroom, Kelela told me that Beyonc\u00e9\u2019s loss at the Grammys depressed her and made her question the metrics of success for mainstream musicians. \u201cIf that\u2019s the highest we can achieve in that framework, it doesn\u2019t even make sense to try,\u201d she told me. A notebook was open to a blank page on the night stand, alongside \u201cB Jenkins,\u201d a Fred Moten poetry book that explores the interstitial space among jazz, black aesthetics and politics. Moten\u2019s work concerns itself with the \u201cresistance of the object\u201d and the way that blackness refuses commodification. Kelela is aware of how artists like her get co-opted, morphed into something symbolic that they no longer control, and is determined to avoid it.\n\nI had already heard the lengths to which she would go to prevent this from happening. The first night we met, I asked her how she managed expectations as an artist in an age of hyperconsumption. I mostly meant her reserve on social media, despite the disturbingly insistent demands in her Twitter and Instagram mentions for her next release. Instead, she described an encounter with Fendi, the Italian luxury brand, which invited her to perform at its new headquarters in Rome to celebrate the start of a new website aimed at millennials. Researching the headquarters online, she discovered that the Palazzo della Civilt\u00e0 Italiana, the impressive white marble colosseum that Fendi had taken over, was built to be a propaganda tool under Benito Mussolini. \u201cI\u2019m Ethiopian,\u201d she reminded me: Mussolini invaded Ethiopia in 1935, just before World War II. She didn\u2019t want her body, her blackness, to be used by a brand, even a big fashion label like Fendi, as a proxy for cool in this context. She asked Fendi representatives to agree to release a statement addressing her concerns as a condition of her involvement. \u201cThat flipped the whole damn script,\u201d she said. They\u2019re still negotiating the partnership, but the interaction speaks to how Kelela views her role: \u201cIf an artist says something, people listen,\u201d she told me. She sees herself as someone who can wield her status as a celebrity to catalyze change.\n\nAs the evening wound down, Kelela invited me to get comfortable and listen to some of her new tracks. She gave me earbuds and left me alone to listen. (When I pressed her about a release date, she made a coquettish face and demurred, saying the songs were still being mixed. In reality, she just signed with Warp Records, which will take over the release of the album.) Earlier in the night, she said that as politically aware as she feels she is, she didn\u2019t make an album that addresses those views. \u201cThere was a period after I finished all the songs, I was nervous because I was like: There\u2019s nothing about my experience as a black woman overtly in this. But I could never not make anything from any other place.\u201d The songs instead deal with the agony of falling out of love and the ecstasy of finding it anew. Her voice is as pretty as ever, rising and crashing like cresting waves over beats that swing from a druggy drone to throbbing bass lines perfect for dance-floor grinding. In their own way, they are a quiet protest: They feel radical in the way a Kerry James Marshall painting or a Ntozake Shange poem expresses the humanity and beauty of black life. \u201cAll of that is happening in a world that does not want me,\u201d she said. \u201cIt is a safe space for us to feel, and not necessarily for anyone else.\u201d\u2666\n\nJenna Wortham is a staff writer for the
